401 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Johnson


NO. COA09-908


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


204 N.C. App. 259; 693 S.E.2d 711; 2010 N.C. App. LEXIS 946


January 12, 2010, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
June 1, 2010, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Forsyth County. Nos. 07 CRS 59440-42, 08 CRS 7128.



DISPOSITION: Reversed, vacated and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following the denial of his motion to suppress, defendant pled guilty to possession of a firearm by a felon, two counts of possession of a stolen firearm, one count of carrying a concealed weapon, and one count of driving while license revoked. Defendant admitted to attaining habitual felon status. The Forsyth County Superior Court (North Carolina) sentenced him to 110 to 141 months' imprisonment. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that the trial court committed error by upholding the warrantless stop of his car based solely on the information provided to police by an anonymous tipster. While the tip at issue included identifying details of a person and car allegedly engaged in illegal activity, if offered few details of the alleged crime, no information regarding the informant's basis of knowledge, and scant information to predict the future behavior of the alleged perpetrator. Because the officers failed to corroborate the tip, the appellate court concluded that the tip lacked sufficient indicia of reliability to justify the warrantless stop. The stop was justified, however based on the officer's knowledge that the driver's license of the registered owner of the vehicle was suspended and the scope of the stop should have been limited to that issue. Defendant also contended that his rights under the Fourth Amendment were violated by the search after his arrest for driving with a revoked license. The appellate court agreed, noting that the search occurred when defendant was on the police car and could not have accessed the passenger compartment of his car.


OUTCOME: The order denying defendant's motion to suppress was reversed. Defendant's convictions for possession of a firearm by a felon, two counts of possession of a stolen firearm, and one count of carrying a concealed weapon were vacated. The case was remanded to the trial court for sentencing as to the driving while license revoked conviction.

 

402 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Pettigrew


NO. COA09-1226


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


204 N.C. App. 248; 693 S.E.2d 698; 2010 N.C. App. LEXIS 951


March 8, 2010, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
June 1, 2010, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion dismissed by State v. Pettigrew, 702 S.E.2d 794, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 824 (N.C., 2010)
Appeal dismissed by State v. Pettigrew, 364 N.C. 439, 706 S.E.2d 467, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 1160 (N.C., Oct. 7, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Forsyth County. No. 07 CRS 61801.



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a jury trial in the Superior Court, Forsyth County, North Carolina, defendant was convicted of two counts of first degree sexual offense and was sentenced to consecutive terms of 32 to 40 years' imprisonment. He appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's half-brother's testified that when he was five and defendant was 11, defendant began sexually abusing him and forcing him to perform sex acts. The molestation continued until the victim turned 10. Defendant contended that there was no evidence that the abuse occurred during the time frame alleged in the bill of particulars. The appellate court disagreed. The victim was 10 years old during the entire time period set out in the indictment and bill of particulars. Although he could not recall the exact date of the last abuse, he testified that he was 10 at the time and that the abuse had been ongoing. Thus, there was substantial evidence that defendant abused him within the time period alleged in the bill of particulars. As defendant was 16 during the time frame when the abuse occurred, the trial court had jurisdiction over the matter under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7B-1604(a) (2009). As sentencing a juvenile to life imprisonment was constitutionally permissible, the fact that defendant was 16 when he committed the crimes did not render his sentence of 32 to 40 years' imprisonment cruel and unusual punishment under U.S. Const. amend. VIII or N.C. Const. art. I, § 27.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

403 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. McCravey


NO. COA09-712


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


203 N.C. App. 627; 692 S.E.2d 409; 2010 N.C. App. LEXIS 722


October 29, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
May 4, 2010, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by State v. McCravey, 364 N.C. 438, 702 S.E.2d 506, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 753 (2010)
Habeas corpus proceeding at McCravey v. Keller, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 45214 (M.D.N.C., Apr. 2, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Forsyth County. No. 07 CRS 060705.



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his convictions for second-degree rape, false imprisonment, and assault inflicting serious injury. He also appealed the order of the Forsyth County Superior Court (North Carolina), which enrolled defendant in lifetime satellite-based monitoring upon completion of his sentences.


OVERVIEW: Defendant testified that sexual intercourse with his estranged spouse was consensual and that he hit her when he believed she was lying about where his cocaine was. He contended that the trial court erred in commenting before the jury that the case was "not about anybody's drug use," in excluding evidence of the spouse's pattern of drug usage, and in ordering defendant's enrollment in lifetime satellite-based monitoring. The court rejected defendant's contentions. The trial court's comment was not prejudicial to defendant because immediately after the comment, defendant's counsel was allowed to ask the spouse about her drug use on the night in question. Defendant's contention regarding exclusion of evidence of the spouse's pattern of drug use was not supported by a specific offer at trial of proof of excluded evidence. Finally, the plain language of "aggravated offense" as defined by N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-208.6(1a) (2007) covered the essential elements of second degree rape, including the use of force, and defendant's conviction for an aggravated offense warranted his lifetime satellite-based monitoring pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-208.40A.


OUTCOME: The court found no error in defendant's convictions or in the order for defendant's lifetime satellite-based monitoring.

405 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Armstrong


NO. COA09-1276


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


203 N.C. App. 399; 691 S.E.2d 433; 2010 N.C. App. LEXIS 653


March 8, 2010, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
April 20, 2010, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion dismissed by, Review denied by State v. Armstrong, 702 S.E.2d 492, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 820 (N.C., 2010)
Review denied by State v. Armstrong, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 1152 (N.C., Oct. 7, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Mecklenburg County. No. 07 CRS 12395-96, 223695.



DISPOSITION: Denial of Defendant's MAR affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Superior Court, Mecklenburg County (North Carolina), convicting him of second-degree murder, a violation of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-17, driving while impaired (DWI), a violation of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 20-138.1, and driving while license revoked (DWLR), a violation of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 20-28, arising out of an automobile crash in which is passenger was killed.


OVERVIEW: While the trial court found juror misconduct violating defendant's constitutional rights, it properly denied a motion for appropriate relief on the ground that the violation was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt. Next, double jeopardy did not apply because the legislature intended to create two separate offenses in DWI and second-degree murder as punishment for second degree murder was controlled by structured sentencing, while punishment for DWI was exempted from the structured sentencing provisions. Next, defendant failed to demonstrate prejudicial error regarding the jury instruction for DWLR. Although the trial court erroneously instructed the jury that the State had proved defendant's knowledge of the suspension, it immediately correctly instructed that the State had the burden of providing defendant had received notice of the suspension. Accordingly, defendant failed to demonstrate prejudicial error. Next, there was no merit to defendant's argument that the trial court erroneously assigned prior record points to his out-of-state convictions. Finally, while the trial court erred in allowing expert testimony on Narcan, the error was harmless in light of the evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

406 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. McRae


NO. COA09-114


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


203 N.C. App. 319; 691 S.E.2d 56; 2010 N.C. App. LEXIS 539


June 10, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
April 6, 2010, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Robeson County. Nos. 05 CRS 57992, 05 CRS 57993, 07 CRS 50814.



DISPOSITION: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pleaded guilty in the Robeson County Superior Court (North Carolina) to possession with the intent to sell and deliver cocaine and other charges. Defendant had moved to suppress all evidence discovered in the search of his jacket and vehicle and statements he made when apprehended. The trial court granted the motion to suppress as to the statements but denied the motion as to the cocaine in defendant's jacket. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: A sheriff's officer received a tip from a reliable, confidential source that defendant, whom he named, would that day be driving a green Grand Am with over 60 grams of cocaine within the city limits. This information was relayed by dispatch. An officer who had heard the dispatch observed a green Grand Am within the city limits and followed it. Defendant turned into a gas station without using his turn signal. The officer pulled up and detained defendant for failure to use his turn signal. When defendant saw other officers approaching, he ran, dropping a jacket containing 56 grams of cocaine. The court held that the failure to use the turn signal gave the officer reasonable suspicion to detain defendant because he was traveling in a through lane with medium traffic and was a short distance in front of the police officer. Therefore, the failure to use a turn signal could have affected another motor vehicle, violating N.C. Gen. Stat. § 20-154(a) (2009). Additionally, the tip from the confidential informant was sufficient to provide reasonable suspicion. He identified defendant by a name the sheriff's officer recognized, described the specific car, and gave other specific information.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction.

 

407 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State HHS v. Armstrong


NO. COA09-639


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


203 N.C. App. 116; 690 S.E.2d 293; 2010 N.C. App. LEXIS 492


October 29, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
March 16, 2010, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Catawba County. No. 07 CVS 3414.
Armstrong v. Cansler, 2009 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 49725 (W.D.N.C., June 1, 2009)



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, the State of North Carolina Department of Health and Human Services, Division of Medical Assistance (DHHS), filed a complaint against various defendants, including two parents and their child (the family), requesting disbursement of settlement funds being held in the Superior Court, Catawba County (North Carolina). The family filed a motion to abate the action, which was denied. The family appealed.


OVERVIEW: The family had recovered a settlement for damages for medical malpractice against certain medical defendants based on the family's daughter's development of cerebral palsy due to alleged negligence during her birth. The family had filed a lawsuit against the secretary of DHHS in federal court, seeking a judgment that DHHS did not have a lien on the proceeds from the minor daughter's personal injury action. The court held that in determining whether the parties and causes were the same for the purpose of abatement by reason of the pendency of the prior action, the test was whether the two actions presented a substantial identity as to parties, subject matter, issues involved, and relief demanded. The parties were the same, and the subject matter of both lawsuits was the funds held by the Catawba County Clerk's office based upon a settlement order and DHHS's lien asserted pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 108A-57. The issues to be determined in the federal lawsuit and in the state action were also substantially the same. Therefore, the trial court erred in denying the family's motion to abate the state court action.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court denying the family's motion to abate the action due to the prior pendency of the federal action.

 

408 of 825 DOCUMENTS


In re W.B.M.


NO. COA009-205


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


202 N.C. App. 606; 690 S.E.2d 41; 2010 N.C. App. LEXIS 372


September 3, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
March 2, 2010, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
New Hanover County. No. 07 JR 219.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner accused filed a motion in the New Hanover County District Court (North Carolina), seeking expunction of his name from the Responsible Individuals List (RIL), alleging that N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7B-323 was unconstitutional. The trial court denied the petition. The trial court also denied the accused's motion to remove his name from the list and rejected his constitutional challenge. The accused appealed.


OVERVIEW: The accused was a child's biological father who had visitation with the child. The child's mother reported troubling behaviors of the child to the county department of social services. After an interview with the accused, he was placed on the RIL of persons identified as being responsible for child abuse or neglect. The accused attempted to expunge and remove his name from the list through the statutory procedures, while challenging the constitutionality of the procedures, but was unsuccessful. The court held that the procedure for placing the accused on the list violated his due process rights under N.C. Const. art. I, § 19. The court noted than an individual could spend more than 169 days on the RIL before the clerk of court calendared an expunction hearing. Although the State had an interest in protecting children, it failed to show that inclusion on the RIL was required for social services or law enforcement to investigate reports of abuse or neglect or remove children from dangerous environments. Due process required the State to substantiate a report of child abuse or neglect by a preponderance of the evidence at a pre-deprivation hearing before adding a person to the RIL.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the orders of the district court and held that the statutory scheme governing placement of individuals on the Responsible Individuals List (RIL) of those determined to have committed child abuse or neglect was unconstitutional.

410 of 825 DOCUMENTS


Munger v. State


NO. COA09-375


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


202 N.C. App. 404; 689 S.E.2d 230; 2010 N.C. App. LEXIS 282


October 26, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
February 16, 2010, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted by Munger v. State, 364 N.C. 435, 702 S.E.2d 303, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 743 (2010)
Review improvidently allowed by Munger v. State, 365 N.C. 3, 705 S.E.2d 734, 2011 N.C. LEXIS 25 (N.C., Feb. 3, 2011)
Motion dismissed by, As moot Munger v. State, 365 N.C. 72, 705 S.E.2d 743, 2011 N.C. LEXIS 84 (N.C., Feb. 3, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Wake County. No. 07 CVS 011756.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In challenges to economic incentive legislation enacted by the North Carolina General Assembly, appellants, three North Carolina taxpaying residents and citizens, sought judicial review of the decision by the Wake County Superior Court (North Carolina) granting an N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1A-1, N.C. R. Civ. P. 12(b)(1) motion to dismiss by appellees, the State of North Carolina, various state officials, and two companies.


OVERVIEW: The citizens argued that they had standing in their capacity as taxpayers to challenge the sales and use tax exemptions granted to eligible internet data centers in the 2006 Current Operations Appropriations Act as violative of the uniformity in taxation provisions of N.C. Const. art. V, § 2(1) and 2(2) and the law of the land clause of N.C. Const. art. I, § 19. The citizens were not eligible to assert traditional taxpayer standing. The mere fact that they paid North Carolina income and sales and use taxes, without more, did not give them standing to challenge the sales and use tax exemption afforded to eligible internet data centers. Next, the trial court did not erroneously conclude that the residents lacked standing to pursue their fair tax claims because they were not in the class of taxpayers discriminated against by the sales and use tax exemptions for eligible internet data centers. Each of the three claims on appeal were discrimination-based. Finally, the trial court did not err by viewing the class to which the citizens had to belong as the class attempting to qualify for the exemption rather than the class subject to the tax itself.


OUTCOME: The decision of the trial court was affirmed.

 

411 of 825 DOCUMENTS


Coventry Woods Neighborhood Ass'n v. City of Charlotte


NO. COA09-611


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


202 N.C. App. 247; 688 S.E.2d 538; 2010 N.C. App. LEXIS 200


October 7, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
February 2, 2010, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal dismissed by Coventry Woods Neighborhood Ass'n v. City of Charlotte, 364 N.C. 128, 695 S.E.2d 757, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 363 (2010)
Decision reached on appeal by, Sanctions disallowed by Coventry Woods Neighborhood Ass'n v. City of Charlotte, 713 S.E.2d 162, 2011 N.C. App. LEXIS 1403 (N.C. Ct. App., July 5, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
From Mecklenburg County. No. 08-CVS-3251.
Coventry Woods Neighborhood Ass'n v. City of Charlotte, 2010 N.C. App. LEXIS 156 (N.C. Ct. App., Feb. 2, 2010)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Mecklenburg County Superior Court (North Carolina) entered summary judgment in favor of appellees, a developer, a city, and a city planning commission. Appellants, a neighborhood association and property owners, argued, inter alia that they were entitled to challenge approval of a preliminary plan for a development and that their action was not barred by N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 1-54.1 or 160A-364.1.


OVERVIEW: The trial court incorrectly found that appellants' claims were barred by § 1-54.1 and § 160A-364.1. The particular local ordinance provisions that they attempted to challenge were included in the city's subdivision, rather than its zoning, ordinance. The limitations period relating to challenges to zoning ordinances set out in §§ 1-54.1 and 160A-364.1 did not apply to challenges to the constitutionality of subdivision ordinance provisions of the type at issue in the present case. Appellants argued that Charlotte, N.C., City Code § 20-88(a), resulted in a fundamental denial of due process as guaranteed by the U.S. Const. amend. XIV and N.C. Const. art. I, § 19. Appellants had not cited any authority in support of the proposition that they were entitled to constitutional protection against changes in the treatment of adjoining tracts of property under properly-adopted zoning or subdivision ordinances. They had asserted nothing more than a reliance on the continued existence of the existing legal situation coupled with a mere expectation that no change to which they objected would be made in the use of an abutting 16-acre tract of property.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

 

412 of 825 DOCUMENTS


Brown Bros. Harriman Trust Co. v. Benson


NO. COA09-474


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


202 N.C. App. 283; 688 S.E.2d 752; 2010 N.C. App. LEXIS 201


October 15, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
February 2, 2010, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Notice of appeal dismissed by, Review denied by Brown Bros. Harriman Trust Co. N.A. v. Benson, 364 N.C. 239, 698 S.E.2d 391, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 514 (2010)
Motion denied by Brown Bros. v. Benson, 703 S.E.2d 157, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 936 (N.C., 2010)
Motion denied by Brown Bros. Harriman Trust v. Benson, 703 S.E.2d 157, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 1165 (N.C., Nov. 4, 2010)
Motion denied by Brown Bros. Harriman Trust v. Benson, 703 S.E.2d 157, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 1166 (N.C., Nov. 4, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Mecklenburg County. No. 08 CVS 13456.
Brown Bros. Harriman Trust Co. N.A. v. Benson, 684 S.E.2d 692, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 942 (N.C., 2009)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff trustee brought an action for declaratory relief against defendant trust beneficiaries in the Superior Court, Mecklenburg County (North Carolina), seeking to determine its ability to administer the trust as valid under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 41-23 (2007). The trustee filed a motion for summary judgment, and the beneficiaries filed a cross-motion. The trial court thus granted the trustee's motion, and the beneficiaries appealed.


OVERVIEW: The trustor created a trust, naming the trustee and naming her three children as primary beneficiaries. In accordance with the provisions of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 41-23, the trust was intended to be perpetual, not subject to the rule against perpetuities. The beneficiaries argued that § 41-23 violated section 34 of the Declaration of Rights, N.C. Const. art. I, § 34, which provided that perpetuities and monopolies were contrary to the genius of a free state and were not allowed. The court held that in the historical context of the passage of the North Carolina Constitution, a perpetuity was the attempt to forbid the alienation of lands under any circumstances, and to provide for their descent or disposition in a fixed, unchangeable way. Section 41-23(a) adopted the approach of requiring the existence of the power of alienation rather than requiring that remote future interests vest or terminate within a certain time period. Thus, under § 41-23, a trust may remain valid in perpetuity as long as the appropriate rights of sale or termination are held.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trustees.

 

413 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Blakeman


NO. COA09-699


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


202 N.C. App. 259; 688 S.E.2d 525; 2010 N.C. App. LEXIS 171


November 17, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
February 2, 2010, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition denied by State v. Blakeman, 364 N.C. 242, 698 S.E.2d 656, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 437 (2010)
Motion granted by State v. Blakeman, 2014 N.C. LEXIS 1133 (N.C., Dec. 18, 2014)
Motion dismissed by, As moot State v. Blakeman, 2014 N.C. LEXIS 1125 (N.C., Dec. 18, 2014)
Writ of certiorari dismissed State v. Blakeman, 2014 N.C. LEXIS 1134 (N.C., Dec. 18, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Johnston County. Nos. 07 CRS 54101; 54692; 54693; 54694; 54696; and 8410.



DISPOSITION: No error at trial; Remanded for resentencing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment of the Johnston County Superior Court (North Carolina), which convicted him of statutory sexual offense, indecent liberties, and habitual felon status. The trial court denied his motion to dismiss the aggravating factor submitted to the jury, which found that he took advantage of a position of trust or confidence, including a domestic relationship, to commit the offense, N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-1340.16(d)(15) (2007).


OVERVIEW: The court of appeals held that the trial court did not err by denying defendant's motion to suppress statements he made to law enforcement officers. Although defendant filed a pretrial motion to suppress his statements, he failed to renew his objection at trial. On cross-examination, he elicited extensive testimony about the statements. The trial court did not err in admitting defendant's cross-examination testimony. Defendant did not object to the testimony or assign it as plain error. He made a conclusory allegation that admission of the testimony was plain error but did not argue that admission had a probable effect on the outcome of the trial. The issues the State commented upon during closing argument were pertinent to evidence introduced at trial, to defense counsel's closing argument, or to both. The trial court erred by denying defendant's motion to dismiss the aggravating factor. The evidence was insufficient to establish that defendant took advantage of a position of trust or confidence, including a domestic relationship, to commit the offense. The evidence showed only that the victim "trusted" defendant in the same way she "trusted" any adult parent of a friend.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals found no error in defendant's convictions but remanded for resentencing.

416 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Fletcher


NO. COA09-926


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


202 N.C. App. 107; 688 S.E.2d 94; 2010 N.C. App. LEXIS 95


December 9, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
January 19, 2010, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Moore County. No. 08 CRS 52563.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was indicted for habitual impaired driving, based on the present incident and three previous DWI convictions. Following the denial of his motion to suppress blood test results, he conditionally pled guilty. Defendant appealed the oral order by the Moore County Superior Court (North Carolina) denying his motion to suppress blood test results.


OVERVIEW: Defendant did not question whether he had refused to submit to a test or whether probable cause existed in order to compel a blood test. Therefore, the only issue was whether the police officer's belief was reasonable under the circumstances. Defendant contended that the officer's belief that the delay caused by obtaining a court order would result in the dissipation of his percentage of blood alcohol was unreasonable and not grounded in fact or knowledge. Competent evidence existed to suggest that her belief was reasonable. Defendant had failed multiple field sobriety tests and was unsuccessful at producing a valid breath sample using the Intoximeter at the police station. The trial court had before it competent evidence to support its finding that exigent circumstances existed to take a blood sample without a warrant. The trial court did not err in denying defendant's motion to suppress, and N.C. Gen. Stat. § 20-139.1 was not unconstitutional on its face nor in its application to the present case.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

 

417 of 825 DOCUMENTS



Burton v. Williams


NO. COA09-582


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


202 N.C. App. 81; 689 S.E.2d 174; 2010 N.C. App. LEXIS 93


November 4, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
January 19, 2010, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Durham County. No. 07 CVS 5558.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant home buyer sought judicial review of the Durham County Superior Court's (North Carolina) directed verdict in favor of appellee estate administrator. The buyer argued that the trial court violated his constitutional right to a jury trial by granting the administrator's motion for a directed verdict at the close of the administrator's evidence because the administrator was the party with the burden of proof at trial.


OVERVIEW: The administrator alleged that a promissory note addendum and release were void and unenforceable because (1) the deceased or seller lacked the mental capacity to assent to the addendum and release at the time of their execution, (2) they were procured through undue influence and duress, (3) they were procured through fraud, and (4) they were unsupported by consideration. The release failed to recite any consideration for the new agreement to release the buyer from having to continue to make payments on the promissory note in the event that the seller died prior to the debt being paid off in full. The release, moreover, provided that it reflected the entire understanding of the parties. Thus, according to the understanding of the parties, no consideration was exchanged in support of the new agreement, making it void and unenforceable. Neither in opposition to the motion for a directed verdict nor on appeal did the buyer argue that if allowed, he would have presented evidence showing that the release was, in fact, supported by consideration. The notary public's acknowledgment of the release was not equivalent to an execution of the release under seal.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.


 

418 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Sullivan


NO. COA09-705


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


201 N.C. App. 540; 687 S.E.2d 504; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 2326


November 17, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
December 22, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by State v. Sullivan, 364 N.C. 247, 699 S.E.2d 921, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 506 (2010)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Sullivan v. North Carolina, 131 S. Ct. 937, 178 L. Ed. 2d 754, 2011 U.S. LEXIS 574 (U.S., Jan. 10, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Pender County. No. 08 CRS 1482.



DISPOSITION: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment of the Pender County Superior Court (North Carolina), which convicted him of operating a motor vehicle on a street or highway without the vehicle being registered in violation of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 20-111(1) (2007) and of operating a motor vehicle on a street or highway without having in full force and effect the financial responsibility required by N.C. Gen. Stat. § 20-313 (2007).


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that §§ 20-111 and 20-313 were unconstitutional. The court of appeals held that §§ 20-111 and 20-313 bore a real and substantial relationship to public safety. Therefore, the General Assembly had ample authority under its police power to enact the sections and to make their violation a criminal offense. There were ample public safety justifications for the vehicle registration and financial responsibility requirements. If defendant did not wish to follow the statutory requirements, he could exercise his right to travel in a variety of other ways. If he wished, he could walk, ride a bicycle or horse, or travel as a passenger in an automobile, bus, airplane or helicopter. However, defendant could not operate a motor vehicle on the public highways. The purpose of §§ 20-111 and 20-313 was very clear. Defendant failed to demonstrate how the statutes failed to give him the type of fair notice that was necessary to enable him or anyone else operating a motor vehicle to conform their conduct to the law. Defendant failed to cite any legal authority supporting his arguments that the trial court lacked jurisdiction because he had no contractual relationship with the State.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals found no error.

 

419 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Whitaker


NO. COA08-1406


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


201 N.C. App. 190; 689 S.E.2d 395; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 2198


April 22, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
December 8, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion granted by State v. Whitaker, 690 S.E.2d 528, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 388 (N.C., 2010)
Motion dismissed by, As moot State v. Whitaker, 692 S.E.2d 876, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 27 (N.C., 2010)
Review denied by, Appeal dismissed by State v. Whitaker, 363 N.C. 811, 692 S.E.2d 877, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 38 (2010)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Whitaker v. North Carolina, 130 S. Ct. 3428, 177 L. Ed. 2d 337, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 4774 (U.S., 2010)
Affirmed by State v. Whitaker, 364 N.C. 404, 700 S.E.2d 215, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 737 (N.C., Oct. 8, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Moore County. No. 06 CRS 520292, 06 CRS 520293, 06 CRS 520294, 06 CRS 520296, 06 CRS 52097. 06 CRS 520298, 06 CRS 520299, 06 CRS 5202300, 06 CRS 52289, 06 CRS 52290. Lindsay R. Davis, Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED IN PART and NO ERROR IN PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted on 11 counts of possession of a firearm by a convicted felon, in violation of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-415.1. The Moore County Superior Court (North Carolina) arrested judgment on 10 of the convictions, and sentenced defendant to 18 to 22 months of imprisonment. Defendant appealed, arguing that § 14-415.1 was unconstitutional both on its face and as applied to him.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's primary argument was that the court had to reexamine the holding of the Britt decision upholding the constitutionality of § 14-415.1 in light of the Heller decision. In the Britt decision, Supreme Court of North Carolina declined to hold that § 14-415.1 was unconstitutional on its face. Defendant argued unsuccessfully that the Heller decision required the court to examine the restriction upon his right to keep and bear arms under strict scrutiny and that under the strict scrutiny analysis the 2004 amendment to § 14-415.1 was unconstitutional. Defendant's facial challenge to the constitutionality of § 14-415.1 failed since he had not established that no set of circumstances existed under which § 14-415.1 would be valid. Under the five Britt factors, § 14-415.1 was not unconstitutional as applied to defendant. His arguments that § 14-415.1 violated the prohibition against ex post facto laws and was an unconstitutional bill of attainder had been considered and rejected by the court. Since the trial court had arrested judgment on 10 of the convictions and the State could reindict defendant, he could be placed in double jeopardy.


OUTCOME: Defendant's 10 convictions for possession of a firearm by a felon where judgment was arrested were reversed. His remaining conviction upon which he was sentenced was affirmed.

 

420 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Veazey


NO. COA09-566


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


201 N.C. App. 398; 689 S.E.2d 530; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 2234


October 27, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
December 8, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by State v. Veazey, 363 N.C. 811, 692 S.E.2d 876, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 67 (N.C., Jan. 28, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Stokes County. No. 06 CRS 050005. L. Todd Burke, Judge.
State v. Veazey, 191 N.C. App. 181, 662 S.E.2d 683, 2008 N.C. App. LEXIS 1228 (2008)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pleaded guilty to driving without a valid license and driving while impaired (DWI) in the Stokes County Superior Court (North Carolina). He appealed the denial of his motion to suppress. The denial was reversed and the case remanded for consideration of the legality of the checkpoint at which defendant was stopped. On remand, the superior court again denied defendant's motion to suppress, and defendant again appealed.


OVERVIEW: A state trooper and another officer set up a traffic checkpoint. The trooper's purpose was to "to enforce any kind of motor vehicle law violations" he might encounter. Defendant stopped at the checkpoint, and the trooper detected a strong order of alcohol coming from the vehicle and noticed that defendant's eyes were red and glassy. When he asked defendant to pull over, defendant ran over an informational sign. He admitted drinking several beers. The court held that defendant failed to challenge the findings of fact of the superior court; defendant merely urged the court to reweigh the factors regarding the reasonableness of the checkpoint. The court could not reweigh the factors as long as they were supported by the evidence, which they were. The primary purpose of the checkpoint was to determine if drivers were duly licensed and observing the motor vehicle laws of North Carolina. The trooper was aware of numerous violations from traffic in that area in the past. The trooper stopped every vehicle at the checkpoint. The trial court's conclusion that the checkpoint was constitutional was supported by the evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court.

422 of 825 DOCUMENTS



Duplin County Bd. of Educ. v. Duplin County Bd. of County Comm'rs


NO. COA09-397


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


201 N.C. App. 113; 686 S.E.2d 169; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 1846


October 1, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
November 17, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Duplin County. 08 CVS 741.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant county board of county commissioners challenged a judgment of the Duplin County Superior Court (North Carolina), which ordered appropriation to plaintiff county board of education's local current expense fund. The board of county commissioners argued that the trial court lacked subject matter jurisdiction due to the board of education's failure to comply with N.C. Gen. Stat. § 115C-431 (2007) and that § 115C-431 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The board of education submitted a budget request to the board of county commissioners, which appropriated an amount less than the budget request. The court of appeals held that the board of education complied with all the applicable statutory requirements, and the trial court was not deprived of subject matter jurisdiction when the board of county commissioners appropriated an additional sum to the board of education. Following the adoption of its budget ordinance, the board of education adopted a resolution that the amount appropriated to it for local current expense and capital outlay was insufficient to support a system of free public schools in the county. The parties took part in a joint public meeting and several mediation sessions but failed to reach an agreement. The board of education complied with the time limits of § 115C-431(c) by filing its complaint within five days after an announcement of no agreement by the mediator. Under § 115C-431(c), a school board had to present evidence of the amount of money it needed to maintain its school system and the amount it needed from the county in order to have the necessary amount.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals affirmed the trial court's decision.


 

423 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Mello


NO. COA08-1054


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


200 N.C. App. 561; 684 S.E.2d 477; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 1708


April 7, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
November 3, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at State v. Mello, 684 S.E.2d 483, 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 1709 (N.C. Ct. App., 2009)
Affirmed by State v. Mello, 364 N.C. 421, 700 S.E.2d 224, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 736 (N.C., Oct. 8, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Forsyth County. Nos. 06 CRS 060161-62.



DISPOSITION: Judgment vacated, reversed and remanded in part, and dismissed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted by a jury in Forsyth County Superior Court (North Carolina) under Winston-Salem, N.C., City Ordinance § 38-29 (Ordinance) for loitering for the purpose of engaging in drug-related activity. Defendant argued that the ordinance was unconstitutional on grounds of overbreadth and vagueness.


OVERVIEW: The indictment alleged that defendant violated § 38-29(b)(4) and (7). The Ordinance did not require proof of intent to violate a drug law, but imposed liability solely for conduct that manifested such purpose. The State's assertion that the present court upheld similar language in the Evans decision had no merit. The Ordinance did not require proof of specific criminal intent. It listed seven types of actions, each of which, by definition, was conduct that manifested a purpose to violate a drug law. Because the Ordinance failed to require proof of intent, it attempted to curb drug activity by criminalizing constitutionally permissible conduct. The Ordinance was unconstitutionally overbroad. The Ordinance also failed to define what type of conduct violated § 38-29(b)(4) and left ordinary persons uncertain on how to adhere to the law. That section was unconstitutionally vague, and it violated the Fourth Amendment. Defendant's argument that the trial court erred in denying his motion to suppress evidence was not properly preserved for appellate review. Defendant also had not preserved his reasonable suspicion challenge to a stop by a police officer.


OUTCOME: Defendant's assignment of error that the trial court erred in admitting evidence of an officer's traffic stop of defendant was dismissed. The trial court's denial of defendant's motion to dismiss the charge of loitering for the purpose of engaging in drug-related activity was reversed, and his conviction was vacated. The case was remanded for resentencing.

 

424 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Vogt


NO. COA08-1441


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


200 N.C. App. 664; 685 S.E.2d 23; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 1738



November 3, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion denied by State v. Vogt, 702 S.E.2d 800, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 1147 (N.C., Oct. 7, 2010)
Affirmed by State v. Vogt, 364 N.C. 425, 700 S.E.2d 224, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 740 (2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
MECKLENBURG County No. 06 CrS 236346.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Mecklenburg County Superior Court (North Carolina) convicted defendant on a guilty plea of taking indecent liberties with a minor and sentenced him as a recidivist to 15-18 months imprisonment, suspended. The trial court also placed defendant on supervised probation and ordered him to register as a sex offender and to enroll in a satellite-based monitoring program for life under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-208.40. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, defendant contended that the trial court erred by subjecting him to lifetime satellite monitoring on the grounds that the date upon which he committed the offense antedated the effective date of § 14-208.40 and that he received constitutionally deficient representation from his trial counsel because she failed to argue that subjecting him to lifetime satellite monitoring violated his federal and state constitutional rights against the enactment of ex post facto laws. Relying on a recent decision of another panel of the court of appeals, the appellate court rejected defendant's argument. Lifetime satellite-based monitoring was intended to be a civil and regulatory, rather than a punitive, regime. Thus, it was not an automatic result of defendant's guilty plea, unlike a mandatory minimum sentence or an additional term of imprisonment, so the fact that he was not advised that he might have been subjected to lifetime satellite-based monitoring at the time of guilty plea did not serve to invalidate his conviction. Additionally, the court was not persuaded that it should have exercised its discretion to take judicial notice of the interim guidelines.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order subjecting defendant to lifetime satellite-based monitoring.

Libertarian Party of N.C. v. State


NO. COA08-1413


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


200 N.C. App. 323; 688 S.E.2d 700; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 1681


April 20, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
October 20, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion granted by Libertarian Party v. State, 689 S.E.2d 876, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 1391 (N.C., 2009)
Later proceeding at Libertarian Party of N.C. v. State, 363 N.C. 805, 690 S.E.2d 700, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 31 (2010)
Affirmed in part and modified in part by Libertarian Party v. State, 2011 N.C. LEXIS 142 (N.C., Mar. 11, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Wake County. No. 05 CVS 13073.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, Libertarian party and candidates, and intervenors, members of the North Carolina Green party, challenged the Wake County Superior Court, North Carolina's determination that N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 163-96(a)(1)-(2) and 163-97.1 did not violate N.C. Const. art. I, §§ 1, 10, 12, 14, and 19, or N.C. Const. art. VI, §§ 1 and 6.


OVERVIEW: The party's current status as a recognized political party through the 2012 general election did not exempt it from its obligation to continue to satisfy the requirement of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 163-96(a)(1) in order to retain its recognition, or from its obligation to satisfy the two percent petition requirement of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 163-96(a)(2) in the event that it was unable to retain its recognition as a political party. Therefore, the party's appeal was not moot. While North Carolina's ballot access scheme inevitably burdened the associational rights of members of small parties as well as the informational interests of all voters regardless of their party affiliation, it also found that associational rights were not absolute and were necessarily subject to qualification if elections were to be run fairly and effectively. Because a legislative enactment had to be upheld unless its unconstitutionality clearly appeared beyond a reasonable doubt or it could not be upheld on any reasonable ground, the appellate court held that N.C. Gen. Stat. § 163-96(a)(2) was not violative of N.C. Const. art. I, §§ 12 or 14, or of the "law of the land" clause of N.C. Const. art. I, § 19.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the superior court was affirmed.

 

426 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Mangino


NO. COA08-1555


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


200 N.C. App. 430; 683 S.E.2d 779; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 1644


August 18, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
October 20, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal dismissed by State v. Mangino, 364 N.C. 621, 705 S.E.2d 378, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 1072 (2010)
Review denied by State v. Mangino, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 1206 (N.C., Dec. 15, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Mecklenburg County. No. 07 CR 24475.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an implied-consent case, the State appealed a holding by the Superior Court, Mecklenburg County (North Carolina) that N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 20-38.6(f) (2007) and 20-38.7(a) (2007) were unconstitutional on various grounds including separation of powers and substantive due process, and defendant argued that the superior court erred by not holding the statutes unconstitutional on equal protection and alternative substantive due process grounds.


OVERVIEW: The challenged statutes did not violate the substantive due process, procedural due process, or equal protection clauses of the State and Federal Constitutions. Pursuant to the Fowler decision, N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 20-38.6(f) (2007) and 20-38.7(a) (2007) did not violate the substantive due process, procedural due process, or equal protection clauses of the State and Federal Constitutions. On the face of the relevant state constitutional provisions alone, N.C. Const. art. IV, §§ 1, 13(2), and 12(3), (4), and (6), the General Assembly acted within its constitutional authority by enacting the challenged statutes that prescribed the jurisdiction of the district and superior courts, and provide a system of appeal from district to superior court. That statutory mechanism governed the procedure or practice for implied-consent offenses in the trial courts of North Carolina, as the General Assembly was constitutionally authorized to do by article IV, § 13. Accordingly, the superior court erred by ruling that the challenged statutes violated the separation of powers provision of the North Carolina Constitution.


OUTCOME: The decision of the superior court was reversed.

King v. Beaufort County Bd. of Educ.


NO. COA08-1038


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


200 N.C. App. 368; 683 S.E.2d 767; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 1640


February 11, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
October 20, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Hardy v. Beaufort County Bd. of Educ., 685 S.E.2d 550, 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 1839 (N.C. Ct. App., 2009)

Motion granted by King v. Beaufort Co. Bd. of Educ., 690 S.E.2d 275, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 394 (N.C., 2010)
Motion granted by King v. Beaufort Co. Bd. of Educ, 690 S.E.2d 275, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 395 (N.C., 2010)
Motion granted by King v. Beaufort Co. Bd. of Educ., 690 S.E.2d 529, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 23 (N.C., 2010)
Motion granted by King v. Beaufort Co. Bd. of Educ., 690 S.E.2d 529, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 25 (N.C., 2010)
Motion granted by King v. Beaufort Co. Bd. of Educ., 693 S.E.2d 655, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 567 (N.C., 2010)
Reversed by, Remanded by King v. Beaufort County Bd. of Educ., 364 N.C. 368, 704 S.E.2d 259, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 733 (2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Beaufort County No. 08 CVS 240.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff minor sued defendants, a board of education and a superintendent, alleging her suspension from school violated her constitutional rights. The trial court dismissed the complaint. The minor appealed.


OVERVIEW: The minor was suspended for 10 days because of her involvement in a fight at school. Additionally, the principal recommended to the superintendent a long-term suspension for the minor for the remainder of the school year. The superintendent followed this recommendation and suspended the minor for the remainder of the school year. The minor alleged defendants' failure to provide an alternative education program for a student given a long-term suspension violated her constitutional right to a free public education. The appellate court found that the disposition of students who had been expelled or suspended long term was ultimately a decision involving the administration of the public schools, a decision which was best left to the legislature. The legislature had clearly considered the issue of alternative education for students who were either suspended long term or expelled, and it did not make access to alternative education mandatory. Under the circumstances, the minor was without an adequate administrative remedy and her claim was properly before the court.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

428 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Morrow


NO. COA08-867


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


200 N.C. App. 123; 683 S.E.2d 754; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 1607


February 11, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
October 6, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by, Stay denied by State v. Morrow, 363 N.C. 747, 689 S.E.2d 372, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 1284 (2009)
Stay denied by State v. Morrow, 363 N.C. 809, 692 S.E.2d 393, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 114 (2010)
Motion denied by State v. Morrow, 364 N.C. 439, 702 S.E.2d 792, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 850 (N.C., Oct. 7, 2010)
Affirmed by State v. Morrow, 364 N.C. 424, 700 S.E.2d 224, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 729 (N.C., Oct. 8, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Wilkes County. Nos. 06 CRS 2364, 06 CRS 50300.



DISPOSITION: Remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was ordered by the Superior Court, Wilkes County, North Carolina to enroll in satellite-based monitoring (SBM) for seven to ten years pursuant to his no contest plea to indecent liberties with a child. Defendant argued, inter alia, that requiring SBM enrollment on the basis of crimes committed before enactment of the SBM statutory scheme violated the Ex Post Facto Clause of the United States Constitution.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that the satellite-based monitoring (SBM) enrollment statutory scheme violated the Ex Post Facto Clause because it increased the punishment for a crime after the crime was committed. The appellate court found that although defendant argued that because mandatory GPS monitoring did not exist on the date he committed the underlying offense, the SBM statute, N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-208.40B (2007), violated the Ex Post Facto Clause of the United States Constitution by increasing his permissible punishment after the offense was committed, the appellate court had carefully considered and overruled an identical challenge to the SBM statute in State v. Bare, N.C. App., 677 S.E.2d 518, 531 (2009), and Bare controlled the instant case. With respect to defendant's argument that the trial court erred by ordering him to enroll in SBM for an indefinite period of time, seven to ten years, the appellate court found that in the absence of any statutory provisions to determine when an offender's monitoring would end if his "period of time" was a range of time, N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-208.40B(c) required the trial court to set a definite time period for defendant's enrollment in SBM.


OUTCOME: The appellate court remanded the trial court order requiring defendant to enroll in satellite-based monitoring (SBM) for further findings of fact regarding whether defendant required the highest possible level of supervision and monitoring, and if so, for the trial court to determine a definite time period for which defendant should have been required to enroll in SBM.

 

429 of 825 DOCUMENTS


Everhart v. O'Charley's, Inc.


NO. COA08-1454


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


200 N.C. App. 142; 683 S.E.2d 728; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 1627


April 23, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
October 6, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Forsyth County. No. 07 CVS 1797.



DISPOSITION: No Error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant corporation appealed a judgment entered following a bifurcated trial in which plaintiff patron was awarded $ 10,000 in compensatory damages in the first phase of the trial and $ 350,000 in punitive damages in the second phase. The Forsyth County Superior Court (North Carolina) subsequently reduced the punitive damages award to $ 250,000. The corporation only challenged the punitive damages award.


OVERVIEW: The primary contention of the corporation was that its judgment notwithstanding the verdict motion should have been granted for insufficient evidence that the patron's injuries were related to willful or wanton conduct attributable to the corporation; however, ample evidence supported the jury's verdict. The testimony elicited by the corporation's counsel would have permitted the jury to draw the favorable inference that once the corporation had notice of an incident, it would take corrective measures to ensure that such an incident would not happen again, thus negating the need to impose punitive damages to deter further misconduct. The patron was entitled to attempt to rebut this inference by showing that the corporation, when it received notice of similar allegations on a prior occasion, did not advise its regional operations directors of those allegations. The evidence at trial, viewed in the light most favorable to the patron, was sufficient to permit the jury to reasonably conclude that the assistant manager's refusal, pursuant to corporate policy, to find out what the patron ingested and learn what first aid was necessary was related to the patron's injuries.


OUTCOME: The punitive damages award was upheld.

 

430 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Mueller


NO. COA09-219


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 1629


June 11, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
October 6, 2009, Filed


NOTICE:

PURSUANT TO RULE 32(b), NORTH CAROLINA RULES OF APPELLATE PROCEDURE, THIS DECISION IS NOT FINAL UNTIL EXPIRATION OF THE TWENTY-ONE DAY REHEARING PERIOD. THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED OPINION. PLEASE REFER TO THE NORTH CAROLINA RULES OF APPELLATE PROCEDURE FOR CITATION OF UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at State v. Mueller, 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 2723 (N.C. Ct. App., Oct. 6, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] 
Hoke County. Nos. 03 CRS 2312, 2313, 2315, 2316, 2318, 2319.

Goldston v. State


NO. COA08-754


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


199 N.C. App. 618; 683 S.E.2d 237; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 1564


January 28, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
September 15, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Stay granted by Goldston v. State, 363 N.C. 802, 687 S.E.2d 685, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 1167 (2009)
Review denied by, Appeal dismissed by Goldston v. State, 363 N.C. 802, 690 S.E.2d 536, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 40 (2010)
Motion granted by Goldston v. State, 693 S.E.2d 914, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 193 (N.C., 2010)
Motion granted by Goldston v. State, 695 S.E.2d 750, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 306 (N.C., 2010)
Motion granted by Goldston v. State, 695 S.E.2d 753, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 311 (N.C., 2010)
Motion granted by Goldston v. State, 701 S.E.2d 683, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 698 (N.C., Sept. 1, 2010)
Affirmed by, By an equally divided Court at Goldston v. State, 364 N.C. 416, 700 S.E.2d 223, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 728 (N.C., Oct. 8, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Wake County. No. 02 CVS 015253.
Goldston v. State, 361 N.C. 26, 637 S.E.2d 876, 2006 N.C. LEXIS 1300 (2006)



DISPOSITION: Reversed in part; affirmed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff citizens filed an action against defendants, the State and the Governor, alleging that transfers by the Governor and the General Assembly from the North Carolina Highway Trust Fund to the General Fund of the State were unlawful diversions of Trust Fund assets and violated N.C. Const. art. V, § 5. They challenged an order of the Wake County Superior Court (North Carolina), which granted the State and Governor summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: N.C. Gen. Stat. § 105-187.9(2) (2007) directed that a portion of the Trust Fund be transferred each year to the General Fund. The court of appeals held that one of the objects of the Trust Fund was to supplement the General Fund. Therefore, use of the Trust Fund monies for that purpose was not a "raid" of the Trust Fund for purposes not previously sanctioned by the General Assembly. The transfer of $ 80,000,000 by the Governor from the Trust Fund to the General Fund exceeded his constitutional authority under N.C. Const. art. III, § 5. Section 5 was a grant of authority to the Governor, which was limited to escrowing or reducing budgeted expenditures and did not create a power to transfer and spend funds appropriated for one purpose to another without statutory authority. The Current Operations and Capital Improvements Appropriations Act of 2001 was not followed in the transferring of the money because the Governor transferred it directly into the General Fund without awaiting legislative action. While the Governor could "escrow" the Trust Fund monies to prevent a deficit, he could not transfer appropriated Trust Fund monies without awaiting appropriate legislative authority.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals revered in part the trial court's order denying the citizens' motion for summary judgment and granting the State and Governor summary judgment. It affirmed the decision of the trial court with regard to the $ 125,000,000 statutory transfer by the General Assembly.

State v. Wagoner


COA08-982


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


199 N.C. App. 321; 683 S.E.2d 391; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 1500


January 28, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
September 1, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended November 22, 2011
Motion to strike denied by State v. Wagoner, 689 S.E.2d 377, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 1356 (N.C., 2009)
Motion denied by State v. Wagoner, 364 N.C. 441, 702 S.E.2d 800, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 849 (N.C., Oct. 7, 2010)
Affirmed by State v. Wagoner, 364 N.C. 422, 700 S.E.2d 222, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 735 (N.C., Oct. 8, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Wilkes County. No. 95CRS2699-2700, 04CRS52252.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pleaded no contest to attempted first degree sex offense, indecent liberties, and indecent liberties with a child. He pleaded guilty to committing a crime against nature and indecent liberties. He challenged an order of the Superior Court, Wilkes County (North Carolina), which required him to enroll in satellite-based monitoring (SBM) pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-208.40B (2007) because he was found to be a recidivist.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the trial court violated his constitutional rights in violation of the prohibition against ex post facto punishments and to be free from double jeopardy. The court of appeals held that defendant's enrollment in SBM did not violate prohibitions against ex post facto law or double jeopardy. The legislature did not intend SBM to be a criminal punishment. Mere involvement of the criminal process in aid of a statutory regime did not render the statutory scheme itself punitive. The 2007 amendment to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-208.42 made a substantive change. The requirements of SBM were different from the conditions of unsupervised probation required by the 2006 statute. The amendment established a different way of maintaining SBM that was not merely a "cosmetic" change. The involvement of courts of law and superior court judges did not indicate a punitive legislative intent. The superior courts had jurisdiction regarding many different types of civil matters. Defendant had not been prosecuted a second time but contended that he was subjected to additional punishments. Because SBM was a civil regulatory scheme and not a punishment, double jeopardy did not apply.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals affirmed the trial court's order requiring defendant to enroll in SBM.

 

434 of 825 DOCUMENTS


Lawyer v. City of Elizabeth


NO. COA08-765


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


199 N.C. App. 304; 681 S.E.2d 415; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 1367


January 14, 2008, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
August 18, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Lawyer v. City of Elizabeth City N.C., 363 N.C. 855, 694 S.E.2d 389, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 223 (N.C., Mar. 11, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Pasquotank County. No. 06 CVS 552.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following the demolition of a structure on their property, plaintiff property owners filed a complaint against defendant city in the Pasquotank County Superior Court, alleging claims of 1) unconstitutional taking without just compensation, 2) destruction of property, 3) violation of due process, 4) trespass, and 5) denial of equal protection. The trial court granted the city's motion for summary judgment, and the owners appealed.


OVERVIEW: The owners purchased the property at a sheriff's sale in 2003 but did not record the deed until 2005. In 2004, the city condemned the property as unfit for human habitation. The city mailed notices to the former owners and posted a notice of condemnation on the property. The former owners wrote to the city that they no longer owned the property and it had been sold at a sheriff's sale. In January 2005, the structure was demolished. The former owners appeared as the owners of record in both the Register of Deeds and Tax Department offices. However, the owners' address was listed on the tax bill. The owners argued that the former owners' letter should have alerted the city that there were other owners of the property who required notice. Under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 160A-443 (2007), the city was required to have procedures to give notice to the owners and interested parties, as defined in N.C. Gen. Stat. § 160A-442 (2007). Although there was no genuine issue of fact as to what steps the city took in attempting to ascertain to whom they should send notice, reasonable minds could differ as to whether the steps taken were sufficient. Therefore, the city was not entitled to summary judgment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the grant of summary judgment in favor of the city.

In re Hayes


NO. COA08-894


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


199 N.C. App. 69; 681 S.E.2d 395; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 1382


January 29, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
August 18, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Stay granted by In re Hayes, 363 N.C. 653, 681 S.E.2d 786, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 866 (2009)
Review denied by In re Hayes, 363 N.C. 803, 690 S.E.2d 694, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 71 (2010)
Stay dissolved by In re Hayes, 363 N.C. 803, 690 S.E.2d 695, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 121 (2010)
Stay denied by In re Hayes, 363 N.C. 803, 690 S.E.2d 695, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 292 (N.C., Jan. 28, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Forsyth County. No. 89 SPC 377.
In re Hayes, 151 N.C. App. 27, 564 S.E.2d 305, 2002 N.C. App. LEXIS 648 (2002)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent patient challenged an order of the Forsyth County Superior Court (North Carolina), which recommitted him to involuntary inpatient treatment after concluding that he failed to prove by a preponderance of the evidence that he was no longer mentally ill or dangerous to others pursuant to the Mental Health, Developmental Disabilities, and Substance Abuse Act of 1985, N.C. Gen. Stat. § 122C-276.1 (2007).


OVERVIEW: Respondent was found not guilty by reason of insanity following a murder trial. He was involuntarily committed to a state mental health facility. He argued that the Act authorized a conditional release as a dispositional option. The court of appeals held that because the trial court was unaware that it had the option of conditionally releasing respondent, it made its findings of fact and conclusions of law under a misapprehension of the law. The Act, N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 122C-264, -268.1, -276.1, and -277 (2007), established the trial court's authority to order a conditional release of an insanity acquittee as a dispositional option in §§ 122C-268.1 and 122C-276.1 hearings. The trial court found that there was a reasonable probability that respondent's seriously violent conduct would be repeated and that he would be dangerous to others in the future if unconditionally released. The trial court did not make findings of fact that such a reasonable probability existed in the absence of an unconditional release. The court of appeals could not determine that the trial court, if aware that a conditional release was a legal disposition, would still have recommitted respondent.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals reversed the trial court's commitment order and remanded the case for a hearing de novo to decide whether respondent met his burden of proof, and if he had, whether he would be conditionally or unconditionally released. It left to the trial court's discretion whether to base the new decision on the existing record or whether to hear additional evidence given the parties' focus on recommitment versus unconditional release.

Wein II, LLC v. Porter


NO. COA08-1033


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


198 N.C. App. 472; 683 S.E.2d 707; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 1357


February 24, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
August 4, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Buncombe County. No. 07 CVS 01555.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owner appealed from a judgment of the Buncombe County Superior Court, North Carolina, which granted summary judgment in favor of defendants and held that the restrictive covenants were binding on plaintiff.


OVERVIEW: This appeal arose from the parties' disagreement about the application and interpretation of restrictive covenants recorded in a consent judgment. Plaintiff sought a declaration that the covenants contained in the consent judgment, and in specific, the limitation on placement of an entryway, were unenforceable and improper in all respects and asserted various grounds for a declaration that the restrictive covenants were not enforceable. Defendants brought a counterclaim for a declaration that the restrictive covenants were valid and binding on plaintiff, including the covenants addressing the siting of a driveway on the property. The instant court found that the restrictive covenants did not forbid plaintiff from siting its driveway on a particular public road. Nor did the covenants state that plaintiff could not build a driveway off the same road that connected defendants' neighborhood with Weaverville Road. The covenants did not state that the only location plaintiff may place a driveway was at Location A.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed in part and reversed in part. Plaintiff, and subsequent owners, must comply with the terms and conditions of the consent judgment. The restrictive covenants did not forbid plaintiff from constructing a driveway.

State v. Worley


NO. COA08-1532


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


198 N.C. App. 329; 679 S.E.2d 857; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 1167


May 20, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
July 21, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended August 18, 2009.
Petition withdrawn by State v. Worley, 363 N.C. 589, 684 S.E.2d 159, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 791 (N.C., Aug. 27, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Buncombe County. No. 05 CRS 64254, 06 CRS 51270.
State v. Worley, 677 S.E.2d 540, 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 757 (N.C. Ct. App., 2009)



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted, upon a guilty plea, of taking indecent liberties with a child. Defendant appealed a judgment entered by the Buncombe County Superior Court (North Carolina), which convicted him of willfully failing to comply with the change of address requirements applicable to registered sex offenders under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-208.11(a)(2), and his sentence to 107 to 138 months of imprisonment, after the denial of his motion to dismiss.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court held that defendant was residing in public housing when the person he was staying with was evicted due to defendant's status as a sex offender. The locks on the apartment were changed, and defendant stopped living in the public housing apartment. Defendant left the public housing apartment on or before August 10, 2005 and failed to report a new address until September 16, 2005. Defendant's claim that he did not have a permanent address, and that he was not required to register a temporary address was rejected. N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 14-208.9(a) and 14-208.11(a)(2) operated on the premise that everyone, at all times, had an address. There was ample support for the verdict convicting defendant of failing to provide timely notice of his change of address in violation of § 14-208.11(a)(2). It was not error to deny defendant's motion to dismiss at the close of the evidence. Finally, defendant did not preserve his void for vagueness challenge to §§ 14-208.9(a) and 14-208.11(a)(2).


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Hoke County Bd. of Educ. v. State


NO. COA08-1036


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


198 N.C. App. 274; 679 S.E.2d 512; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 1178


March 26, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
July 21, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Hoke County Bd. of Educ. v. State, 363 N.C. 653, 686 S.E.2d 515, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 1077 (2009)
Subsequent appeal at, Petition denied by Hoke County Bd. of Educ. v. State, 365 N.C. 552, 724 S.E.2d 510, 2012 N.C. LEXIS 175 (2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Wake County. No. 95 CVS 1158.
Hoke County Bd. of Educ. v. State, 358 N.C. 605, 599 S.E.2d 365, 2004 N.C. LEXIS 886 (2004)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action brought by plaintiff local boards, and others, against defendant state board, the Wake County Superior Court (North Carolina) found that the state board failed in its constitutional duty to provide a sound basic education in rural districts, and directed the state board to remedy deficiencies. Later, the trial court denied a motion for attorneys' fees and costs filed by the local boards. The local boards appealed.


OVERVIEW: The trial court's N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1A-1, N.C. R. Civ. P. 54(b) certification stated that, inter alia, the interlocutory denial of the motion for attorneys' fees was an ancillary matter and did not affect the on-going proceedings in the case. The appellate court found that this appeared to have been the exact opposite of what was necessary to establish a substantial right required for an interlocutory appeal. Nevertheless, the appellate court elected to invoke its N.C. R. App. P. 2 power to hear this appeal notwithstanding the fact that the order was interlocutory. The state took no affirmative actions against the local boards, and the state's failure to act was not be extrapolated into "state action" for purposes of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 6-19.1 (2007). Thus, the trial court did not err in finding that § 6-19.1 did not apply. Neither the common fund doctrine nor the substantial benefit doctrine were applicable. None of the theories upon which the local boards relied supported an award of attorneys' fees, and the trial court's order was without error.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

In re D.L.H.


NO. COA08-1019


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


198 N.C. App. 286; 679 S.E.2d 449; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 1175


February 12, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
July 21, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Stay granted by In re D.L.H., 363 N.C. 581, 681 S.E.2d 347, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 727 (2009)
Review granted by, Stay granted by In re D.L.H., 363 N.C. 653, 686 S.E.2d 513, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 1069 (2009)
Reversed by In re D.L.H., 364 N.C. 214, 694 S.E.2d 753, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 421 (N.C., June 17, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Guilford County. No. 03-JB-808.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED IN PART and REVERSED IN PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant juvenile challenged orders of the Guilford County District Court (North Carolina), which required her to serve fourteen days in a juvenile detention center, placed her in the detention center pending disposition, refused to consider her motion seeking release from custody, and entered a Level 2 disposition.


OVERVIEW: The juvenile was adjudicated delinquent. She was placed on probation and sentenced to fourteen days in the detention center. The sentence was stayed. A motion for review was filed, alleging that she violated the terms of her probation. The juvenile was ordered to serve the sentence and was placed in the detention center pending disposition. The court of appeals held that the trial court erred by failing to give the juvenile credit towards her sentence for the time she served in secure custody prior to her dispositional hearing pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15-196.1 (2007). The trial court properly ordered the juvenile to be confined to secure custody pending disposition pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7B-1903(c) (2007). The juvenile had previously been adjudicated as delinquent and admitted to subsequent probation violations. The trial court erred by failing to entertain the juvenile's motion to review the order of secure custody under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7B-1906(b) (2007). A juvenile confined to secure custody pending disposition or placement was entitled to a hearing at intervals of no more than ten calendar days to determine whether continued secure custody was warranted.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals affirmed the orders in part and reversed the orders in part.

State v. Anderson


NO. COA08-1523


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


198 N.C. App. 201; 679 S.E.2d 165; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 1106


May 20, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
July 7, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by State v. Anderson, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 761 (N.C., Oct. 7, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Wilkes County. Nos. 93 CRS 2280-84.



DISPOSITION: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pled guilty to misdemeanor sexual battery in Haywood County, North Carolina. The Superior Court, Wilkes County (North Carolina), then ordered defendant to be subjected to lifetime satellite-based monitoring pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-208.40(a)(1). Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the trial court erred by finding that he was subject to lifetime satellite-based monitoring when he was not advised, before his 1994 guilty plea in Wilkes County to various offenses, and his 2007 guilty plea in Haywood County to misdemeanor sexual battery, that the monitoring might have been imposed as a result of his pleas. However, defendant admitted that the statute as written applied to him and subjected him to lifetime satellite-based monitoring. Defendant did not object at trial to the imposition of the monitoring based on this argument, and thus, the appellate court found that this argument was subject to dismissal pursuant to N.C. R. App. P. 10(b)(1). Further, defendant did not object at the hearing on the grounds that N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-208.40(a)(1) was void for vagueness. In fact, defendant admitted that his conduct constituted recidivist behavior as defined by the statute, and having admitted that he was a recidivist as defined by the statute, he was not permitted to argue on appeal that the provisions for determining recidivism were unconstitutionally vague. The monitoring was not a punishment, and thus did not violate double jeopardy.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.
