State v. Worley


NO. COA08-1532


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


677 S.E.2d 540; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 757


May 20, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
June 16, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Opinion withdrawn by, 07/02/2009
Substituted opinion at State v. Worley, 679 S.E.2d 857, 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 1167 (N.C. Ct. App., 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Buncombe County. No. 05 CRS 64254, 06 CRS 51270.
State v. Worley, 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 1020 (N.C. Ct. App., June 16, 2009)



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury convicted defendant of willfully violating the change of address requirements applicable to registered sex offenders in violation of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-208.11(a)(2). The Buncombe County Superior Court (North Carolina) sentenced defendant to a term of 107 to 138 months incarceration in the custody of the North Carolina Department of Correction. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the trial court erred by denying his motion to dismiss at the close of all evidence. The appellate court disagreed, finding ample evidence to support the jury's verdict convicting defendant of failing to provide timely notice of his change of address, in violation of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-208.11(a)(2). Among other things, the evidence showed that defendant moved from his old address and knew that he only had ten days to notify the sheriff's department of his move, but that defendant did not notify the sheriff's department of his change of address until at least a month later. While defendant argued that he had not established a new "address" until the date upon which his submitted the change of address, the appellate court found that defendant did not intend to return to his old address or hold that location out to the public as his address. After defendant left his old address, the evidence tended to show that he may have traveled from place to place within his hometown. However, there was also substantial evidence in the record tending to show that defendant changed his address to a new location more than 10 days prior to recording the change of address.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

 

445 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Allen


NO. COA08-773


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


197 N.C. App. 208; 676 S.E.2d 519; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 788


January 14, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
May 19, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Person County. No. 07 CRS 50910.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following the denial of defendant's motion to suppress, defendant pled guilty to habitual driving while impaired, reserving her right to appeal the denial of her motion to suppress. The Person County Superior Court (North Carolina) sentenced defendant to a minimum of 12 months and a maximum of 15 months in prison. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the seizure was unreasonable because it was based on an uncorroborated anonymous tip which lacked a sufficient indicia of reliability. The appellate court disagreed. Contrary to defendant's contention, the stop was not based on an anonymous tip from an unknown, unaccountable informant. A victim of the fight/assault provided information to an officer that the offender was a tall white male that had been driven away from the scene in a small, dark car that was operated by a white female with blond hair. The officer's stop was not based solely on the victim's description, but also on the officer's own observations of defendant's hurried actions, the fact that it appeared to the officer that defendant was trying to avoid him, and defendant's proximity to the scene of the assault. Defendant also contends she did not match the tipster's description because she was driving a small light-colored car with a female passenger instead of a small dark car with a male passenger. The fact that the car did not exactly match the description did not render the stop unreasonable and at the time of the stop, the officer could not tell if the passenger was male or female.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

447 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Fowler


NO. COA08-652


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


197 N.C. App. 1; 676 S.E.2d 523; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 832


January 26, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
May 19, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition dismissed by, As moot State v. Fowler, 364 N.C. 129, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 354 (2010)
Review denied by State v. Fowler, 364 N.C. 129, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 409 (N.C., Apr. 14, 2010)
Review denied by, Motion granted by State v. Fowler, 364 N.C. 129, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 408 (N.C., Apr. 14, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Mecklenburg County. No. 07 CRS 200258.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and Remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with willfully operating a motor vehicle while subject to an impairing substance. He filed a pretrial motion pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 20-38.6(a). A district court granted the motion under § 20-38.6(f). The State appealed under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 20-38.7(a). It challenged an order of the Mecklenburg County Superior Court (North Carolina), which found that §§ 20-38.6 and 20-38.7 were unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The court of appeals held that the State had no statutory right of appeal pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-1445(a)(1) from the superior court's interlocutory order. However, it granted the State's petition for writ of certiorari due to the substantial questions at issue. By enacting §§ 20-38.6(a), (f) and 20-38.7(a), the General Assembly created rules that affected the procedure and practice of the superior and district court divisions only, as it was permitted to do under N.C. Const. art. IV, § 13, cl. 2. The superior court erred by concluding that §§ 20-38.6(a), (f) and 20-38.7(a) violated the Former Jeopardy Clause of the United States Constitution. The State's right to appeal a district court's preliminary determination indicating that it would grant a defendant's pretrial motion to suppress or dismiss made in accordance with § 20-38.6(a) did not deprive defendants charged with implied-consent offenses of their guaranteed freedom from former jeopardy. Sections 20-38.6(a), (f), and 20-38.7(a) did not violate the Due Process Clause because they were not unreasonable, arbitrary, or capricious and were substantially related to the valid object sought to be obtained.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals reversed the order and remanded the matter to the superior court with instructions to remand the case to the district court to enter a final order granting defendant's motion to suppress evidence.

Wal-Mart Stores East v. Hinton


NO. COA08-450


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


197 N.C. App. 30; 676 S.E.2d 634; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 809


October 22, 2008, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
May 19, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Related proceeding at Sam's East, Inc. v. Hinton, 676 S.E.2d 654, 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 781 (N.C. Ct. App., 2009)
Motion dismissed by, As moot Wal-Mart Stores East, Inc. v. Hinton, 363 N.C. 748, 689 S.E.2d 375, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 1308 (N.C., Dec. 10, 2009)
Motion dismissed by, As moot Wal-Mart Stores East, Inc. v. Hinton, 689 S.E.2d 375, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 1351 (N.C., Dec. 10, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY: Wake County. No. 06-CVS-3928.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer filed a complaint, pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 105-267, against appellee, the Secretary of Revenue of the State of North Carolina, in the Wake County Superior Court (North Carolina), demanding a refund of taxes paid. The parties filed cross-motions for summary judgment. The trial court entered an order granting the Secretary's motion for summary judgment. The taxpayer appealed.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer was a multistate corporation operating discount retail stores. All real property pertaining to the store premises was transferred from the parent company of the taxpayer to a newly established business trust. An affiliated property company owned all of the voting units of the business trust. Thus, the business trust owned and leased stores within North Carolina, passed along income to the property company received from leasing and subleasing those stores, which the property company further passed along to the taxpayer in the form of dividends. The gravamen of the taxpayer's complaint was that the Secretary of Revenue of the State of North Carolina had no authority to force the combination of the taxpayer with the business trust and the property company for the purpose of reporting taxable income. On appeal, the court found that (1) N.C. Gen. Stat. § 105-130.6 (1999) provided the Secretary with authority to combine the three entities for the purpose of reporting taxable income, (2) § 105-130.6 was constitutional under N.C. Const. art. I, § 16, N.C. Const. art. V, § 2(2) and N.C. Const. art. V, § 2(6), and (3) the Secretary's administration of § 105-130.6 was lawful.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed with respect to the assessment of additional taxes and interest thereon. The assessment of penalties for the taxpayer's understatement of its taxable income was also affirmed.

Sullivan v. Pender County


NO. COA08-1037


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


196 N.C. App. 726; 676 S.E.2d 69; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 526


March 10, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
May 5, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Pender County. No. 07 CVD 1131.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owner, acting pro se, filed an action against defendant county in which he sought a refund of his property tax. The Pender County District Court (North Carolina) denied plaintiff's motion for summary judgment and granted summary judgment for defendant. Plaintiff appealed.


OVERVIEW: The court noted that plaintiff had violated N.C. R. App. P. 28(b) by including argumentative assertions, facts unsupported by reference to the record, and irrelevant facts in his statement of facts and by failing to support his assertions with legal authority. The court, however, would address the merits. Plaintiff's argument that he was not a taxpayer had been rejected in a previous appeal by plaintiff. Nothing supported his argument that property conveyed by bill of sale was not subject to ad valorem taxation. Next, because plaintiff had not included a transcript of a hearing during which the trial court allegedly made certain statements, the court could not reach the merits. The challenged statements, however, appeared to be innocuous, accurate statements of fact. There was no merit to plaintiff's argument that the trial court was unauthorized to enter summary judgment for defendant because defendant had not requested summary judgment. N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1A-1, N.C. R. Civ. P. 56(c) (2007) authorized such a ruling. There was no authority for plaintiff's argument that local property taxes were an unconstitutional taking under U.S. Const. amend. V and N.C. Const. art. I, § 19.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision.

In re J.D.B.


NO. COA08-499


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


196 N.C. App. 234; 674 S.E.2d 795; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 371


January 12, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
April 7, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion granted by In re J.D.B., 678 S.E.2d 230, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 496 (N.C., 2009)
Motion granted by In re J.D.B., 680 S.E.2d 873, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 664 (N.C., 2009)
Affirmed by In re J.D.B., 363 N.C. 664, 686 S.E.2d 135, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 1288 (N.C., Dec. 11, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Orange County. No. 05 JB 115.
In re J.B., 183 N.C. App. 299, 644 S.E.2d 270, 2007 N.C. App. LEXIS 1067 (2007)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a juvenile, was adjudicated a delinquent upon his admission to felonious breaking and entering and larceny. He sought review of the judgment of the Orange County District Court (North Carolina), which, on remand from the court for the trial court to make findings of fact to support its denial of defendant's motion to suppress, filed and entered its order making findings of fact, conclusions of law, and denying the motion to suppress.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a seventh-grader, was escorted into a room at school to be interviewed regarding his involvement in a crime. He was not administered Miranda warnings. During this interview, defendant admitted to entering the houses and taking certain items with another juvenile. Following the interview, police obtained a search warrant and searched defendant's home. The court held that the trial court properly denied defendant's motion to suppress, rejecting defendant's contention that his statement occurred during a custodial interrogation where officers failed to offer the proper warnings under Miranda and N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7B-2101(a) (2005). The court reviewed the trial court's findings of fact and held that a reasonable person in defendant's position would not have believed himself to be in custody or deprived of his freedom of action in some significant way. The trial court found that defendant was not searched or handcuffed, that he began speaking with the investigator only after agreeing to answer questions, that his responses and questions indicated that he understood that he did not have to answer questions, and that he was allowed to leave the school when the bell rang.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

 

451 of 825 DOCUMENTS


Scott & Jones, Inc. v. Carlton Ins. Agency, Inc.


NO. COA08-745


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


196 N.C. App. 290; 677 S.E.2d 848; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 368


November 20, 2008, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
April 7, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Duplin County. No. 06 CVS 903.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant contractor sued appellee insurance agents in the Superior Court, Duplin County (North Carolina), for negligence and breach of contract. The insurance agents filed an answer which included the statute of limitations as an affirmative defense. The insurance agents filed a motion for summary judgment under that defense. The trial court allowed the motion and dismissed with prejudice the contractor's action. The contractor appealed.


OVERVIEW: The contractor relied upon the insurance agents to obtain insurance for the contractor's needs. However, in a personal injury lawsuit, it was determined that products completed operations coverage for the contractor did not exist under an insurance policy for work that the contractor performed on a project. The contractor therefore asserted that the insurance agents failed to obtain the necessary insurance coverage for the contractor's operations. On appeal, the court found that the insurance agents were not providers of professional services for purposes of the extended statute of limitations under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1-15(c) (2005). Therefore, the statute of limitations under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1-52 for general negligence and breach of contract claims was applicable. In addition, the contractor had no valid argument regarding extension of the statute of limitations under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1-52(16) (2005) due to late discovery of the lack of insurance coverage. Thus, the contractor's causes of action were barred by the statute of limitations because its complaint was filed approximately three years and nine months after the latest date that its claims could possibly have accrued.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

452 of 825 DOCUMENTS


Cashwell v. Dep't of State Treasurer, Ret. Sys. Div.


NO. COA08-432


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


196 N.C. App. 80; 675 S.E.2d 73; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 452


October 9, 2008, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
April 7, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Wake County. No. 07 CVS 09625.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner retiree sought review of a determination of respondent director which denied the retiree a tax free pension under the Consolidated Judicial Retirement System (CJRS) and the Teachers' and State Employees Retirement System (TSERS). The Wake County Superior Court (North Carolina) affirmed the director's declaratory ruling. The retiree appealed.


OVERVIEW: In order to be entitled to a tax-free pension, an employee had to be vested in the CJRS and the TSERS, by completing five continuous years of service, as of August 12, 1989. Although the retiree had completed five years of service before that date, he withdrew his contributions from those systems and left public employment. Later, after August 12, 1989, he returned to public employment, and eventually repaid the money he had withdrawn from the CJRS and the TSERS. The retiree argued that his repayment of contributions withdrawn before August 12, 1989 entitled him to a tax-free pension. The appellate court disagreed. The retiree was not vested as of August 12, 1989. Read together, N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 135-4, 135-5 made clear that a member who left state service and withdrew contributions in the retirement system had no rights to any benefits within the system except for the right to repay previously withdrawn contributions as provided in § 135-4. The repayment served only to increase the years of service creditable. Further, the director's interpretation in denying the retiree's request was reasonable and based on a permissible construction of the statutes.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

453 of 825 DOCUMENTS


Malloy v. Cooper


NO. COA08-892


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


195 N.C. App. 747; 673 S.E.2d 783; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 246


December 10, 2008, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
March 17, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Granville County. No. 99 CVS 142.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant humane society sought to intervene in a lawsuit originally brought by appellee owner of a gun club challenging the constitutionality of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-360. Earlier, following appeals and remands, the Superior Court, Granville County (North Carolina), entered a permanent injunction against prosecution of the owner under § 14-360. The trial court denied the humane society's motion to intervene. The humane society appealed.


OVERVIEW: The humane society claimed that, due to an amendment of 15A N.C. Admin. Code 10B.0121, the injunction preventing prosecution of the owner pursuant to § 14-360 should have been dissolved. However, the appellate court found that the humane society failed to file its motion to intervene pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1A-1, N.C. R. Civ. P. 24 (2003), in the appellate court until more than three years after the amendment, and failed to file its motion to intervene in the trial court until nearly three and a half years after the amendment. While the humane society was involved in the original appeal of the case through the filing of an amicus curiae brief, and though the humane society made attempts to persuade the defendants to pursue the matter, the trial court's ruling that the motion to intervene was not timely filed was not so arbitrary that it could not have been the result of a reasoned decision. Further, the humane society did nothing for two years after the amendment until it began pressuring the defendants to move to dissolve the injunction. The humane society's claim that it was unaware an injunction had been entered by the trial court was not persuasive.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

454 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Maynard


NO. COA08-847


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


195 N.C. App. 757; 673 S.E.2d 877; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 263


January 15, 2009, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
March 17, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected March 18, 2009.
Stay granted by State v. Maynard, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 333 (N.C., Apr. 1, 2009)
Review denied by, Stay denied by State v. Maynard, 363 N.C. 259, 677 S.E.2d 165, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 487 (2009)
Stay dissolved by State v. Maynard, 363 N.C. 259, 677 S.E.2d 165, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 500 (2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Nash County. No. 06 CRS 54412, 07 CRS 569.



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After the Nash County Superior Court (North Carolina) denied defendant's motion to dismiss, a jury convicted defendant of keeping more than three dogs at her residence in violation of Nashville, N.C., Ordinance § 4.3. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that § 4.3, limiting the number of dogs that she could keep in her residence, was unconstitutional, and that the restrictions of no more than three dogs over five months of age was inherently arbitrary. The appellate court found, however, that § 4.3 did not set an arbitrary, immutable limit on the number of pets. Rather, the number of dogs allowed was based on the square footage of the lot. Further, there was no restriction on the number of dogs less than five months old. The fact that Nashville, North Carolina chose to regulate by size of lot, number of dogs, and age of dogs rather than by size or breed of dog did not render the ordinance arbitrary and unconstitutional. The record revealed that the town enacted § 4.3 to reduce noise and odor problems within the city limits. These objectives were clearly legitimate public purposes, and a limitation on the number of dogs per lot was directly connected to these objectives. Defendant failed to meet her burden of showing that § 4.3 was arbitrary, unreasonable, and unrelated to the public health and welfare of the citizens of the town. The ordinance was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Town of Pinebluff v. Marts


NO. COA08-434


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


195 N.C. App. 659; 673 S.E.2d 740; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 261


September 23, 2008, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
March 17, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Moore County. No. 06 CVS 1700.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee town sued appellant real estate developers in the Moore County Superior Court (North Carolina), for violation of a zoning ordinance. The town requested an injunction ordering the developers to comply with the ordinance by installing a minipark and reserving open space in a subdivision. The court granted the town's motion for summary judgment and ordered the developers to install a minipark and provide open space. The developers appealed.


OVERVIEW: The town enacted a zoning ordinance which required developers of new residential developments to provide miniparks and open space for the use of their residents. Before the ordinance was adopted, the developers obtained the town's approval and began development of a subdivision. Although the developers initially agreed to comply with the ordinance, they later informed the town that they did not intend to comply with the ordinance. On appeal, the court found that the developers, rather than directly challenging the ordinance, waited to object to the ordinance until after the town sought to enforce it. Accordingly, the developers could not collaterally attack the validity of the ordinance. In addition, the court found that (1) the town was not estopped from enforcing the ordinance against the developers; (2) the town was entitled to seek an injunction pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 160A-375 and 160A-389 (2007); (3) the developers, because they failed to make a showing that enforcement of the injunction would be inequitable, failed to establish any unconstitutional impairment of contract under U.S. Const. art. I, § 10; and (4) the enactment of the ordinance was not a taking.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Herrera


NO. COA08-491


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


195 N.C. App. 181; 672 S.E.2d 71; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 126


October 22, 2008, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
February 3, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by State v. Herrera, 363 N.C. 377, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 997 (2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Durham County. No. 04CRS54317.



DISPOSITION: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted by a jury in the Durham County Superior Court (North Carolina) of first degree murder. The court sentenced defendant to life imprisonment without parole. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a Honduran national, challenged the evidentiary proceedings at his trial. On appeal, the court found that the trial court did not err by denying defendant's motion to suppress because (1) the police did not violate defendant's Fifth Amendment rights by interrogating defendant when the police placed a phone call from an interrogation room to his grandmother to inform her that defendant was going to jail or by then allowing defendant to converse with her on a speaker phone in the presence of a detective and an interpreter; and (2) suppression was not required due to the State of North Carolina's failure to inform defendant of his right to contact the Honduran Consulate. In addition, the trial court did not abuse its discretion under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-910 (2007) in allowing testimony disclosed on the eve of trial when defendant did not request a recess or continuance to address the newly disclosed evidence. Finally, even assuming that the trial court erred in deciding to instruct the jury pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-1235 (2007), given the absence of any indication of deadlock or coercion, any error was not prejudicial to defendant.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

460 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Watkins


NO. COA07-1213


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


195 N.C. App. 215; 672 S.E.2d 43; 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 122


April 15, 2008, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
February 3, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Guilford County. Nos. 04 CRS 66852, 04 CRS 66853.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pled guilty to second degree murder and first degree robbery. Defendant's motion to withdraw his guilty plea, made prior to sentencing, was denied. The Guilford County Superior Court (North Carolina) sentenced defendant to 220 to 273 months for the murder charged and 94 to 122 months for the burglary charge. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued the trial court should have excluded, based on the attorney-client privilege, portions of his former attorney's testimony at the hearing on his motion to withdraw his guilty plea. The appellate court concluded that the subject statements were not privileged, because the information was disclosed so that counsel could provide it to the prosecutor in an attempt to negotiate a plea arrangement. Even assuming the conversation was privileged, its admission did not prejudice defendant given the other evidence of his guilt. Defendant also claimed the trial court erred in refusing to allow him to withdraw his plea. The appellate court disagreed, finding that defendant's delay far exceeded the lapse of time permitted in other cases, and that there was no indication that defendant did not fully understand the consequences of his plea. Finally, the appellate court concluded that there was no error in defendant's sentence, as the prohibition on ex post facto laws was not implicated by application of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-1340.14(c); the increased sentence due to the classification change only enhanced his present sentence, and did not punish defendant for his prior conviction.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

Brinegar v. City of Winston-Salem


NO. COA08-157


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 121


September 24, 2008, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
February 3, 2009, Filed


NOTICE:

PURSUANT TO RULE 32(b), NORTH CAROLINA RULES OF APPELLATE PROCEDURE, THIS DECISION IS NOT FINAL UNTIL EXPIRATION OF THE TWENTY-ONE DAY REHEARING PERIOD. THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED OPINION. PLEASE REFER TO THE NORTH CAROLINA RULES OF APPELLATE PROCEDURE FOR CITATION OF UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at Brinegar v. City of Winston-Salem, 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 873 (N.C. Ct. App., Feb. 3, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] 
Forsyth County. No. 06 CVS 7373.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff former police officer sued defendant city for constitutional violations and breach of contract. The city moved for summary judgment pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1A-1, N.C. R. Civ. P. 56, judgment on the pleadings pursuant to N.C. R. Civ. P. 12(c), and dismissal fo the complaint pursuant to N.C. R. Civ. P. 12(b)(6). The Forsyth County Superior Court (North Carolina) granted the city's motion for summary judgment. The city appealed.


OVERVIEW: The officer suffered from a non-specific, generalized anxiety disorder. A officer's condition worsened after six years on the job and the officer went on sick leave. The officer sought disability retirement benefits. While waiting on a decision from the medical review board, the officer was arrested for attempting to sell marijuana and was suspended pending possible termination. The medical review board determined that the officer would work as a records specialist, but the officer was ineligible at the time of the offer due to his suspension. On appeal, the officer argued that the trial court erred in granting the city's motion for summary judgment in violation of the officer's substantive due process rights. The appellate court disagreed, finding that the officer never had a protected property interest in disability retirement benefits under the applicable city ordinance. The appellate court also agreed with the trial court that the officer's breach of contract claim was barred by the applicable two year statute of limitations, having been filed more than six years after the conducting supporting the claim.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

State v. Wooten


NO. COA08-734


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


194 N.C. App. 524; 669 S.E.2d 749; 2008 N.C. App. LEXIS 2232


October 30, 2008, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
December 16, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by, Writ of certiorari dismissed State v. Wooten, 363 N.C. 138, 676 S.E.2d 308, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 269 (N.C., Mar. 19, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Greene County. No. 02 CRS 50157.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order of the Greene County Superior Court (North Carolina), which enrolled him in satellite-based monitoring (SBM) for the duration of his life following release from custody pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-208.40B.


OVERVIEW: Defendant entered a no contest plea to taking indecent liberties with a minor and was sentenced to a minimum term of 20 months to a maximum term of 24 months imprisonment. Because defendant had a prior conviction for taking indecent liberties with a minor, he was classified as a recidivist under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-208.6(2b). Therefore, the trial court determined that he was subject to SBM. The court of appeals held that the trial court properly exercised jurisdiction in the case. Defendant was a person who fit the criteria the legislature intended for participation in the SBM program. The legislation became effective 16 August 2006. Defendant completed his sentence and was eligible for release, but not eligible for post-release supervision, after the effective date of the legislation. A prior conviction did not have to be reportable to be considered by the trial court when making the recidivism determination. Defendant could not prove either element of his ineffective assistance of counsel claim. Although trial counsel was a bit disorganized and inartfully presented defendant's constitutional arguments, trial counsel's performance did not rise to the level of deficient.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals affirmed the judgment.

State v. Smith


NO. COA08-533


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


193 N.C. App. 739; 668 S.E.2d 612; 2008 N.C. App. LEXIS 2021


October 23, 2008, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
November 18, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Stay granted by State v. Smith, 362 N.C. 687, 670 S.E.2d 903, 2008 N.C. LEXIS 1071 (2008)
Review denied by, Stay denied by State v. Smith, 363 N.C. 588, 684 S.E.2d 37, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 764 (N.C., Aug. 27, 2009)
Stay dissolved by State v. Smith, 363 N.C. 588, 684 S.E.2d 37, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 749 (N.C., Aug. 27, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Lenior County. No. 06 CRS 050777.



DISPOSITION: Vacated and Remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment by the Lenior County Superior Court (North Carolina) that denied his motions to suppress and to dismiss a habitual felon indictment; defendant claimed that his plea was not entered knowingly and voluntarily, that the trial court erred when it denied his motion to suppress, and that his counsel was ineffective.


OVERVIEW: After defendant pleaded guilty to possession with intent to sell and deliver cocaine pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 90-95(a) and having attained habitual felon status, the trial court sentenced him to a mitigated active sentence of a minimum of 70 months and a maximum of 93 months incarceration. The appellate court found that the record did not establish a knowing and voluntary waiver of jury trials when the guilty plea and admission of habitual felon status were entered on misunderstandings that the denials of defendant's pretrial motions were preserved for appellate review. Based upon defendant's guilty plea, he had a right to appeal only the denial of his motion to suppress. He did not have a right to appeal the denial of his motion to dismiss his habitual felon indictment. Therefore, the appellate court was without authority to review, either as of right or by certiorari under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-1444(e) (2001) and N.C. R. App. P. 21(a)(1) (2003), the denial of defendant's motion to dismiss his habitual felon indictment. Consequently, defendant could not receive the benefit of his bargain based on the laws of the state or the appellate rules.


OUTCOME: The judgment based on defendant's plea was vacated, and the case was remanded for further proceedings.

Copper v. Denlinger


NO. COA07-205


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


193 N.C. App. 249; 667 S.E.2d 470; 2008 N.C. App. LEXIS 1817


September 13, 2007, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
October 21, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion granted by Copper v. Denlinger, 670 S.E.2d 904, 2008 N.C. LEXIS 912 (N.C., 2008)
Motion granted by Copper v. Denlinger, 672 S.E.2d 23, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 2 (N.C., 2009)
Review granted by, in part, Notice of appeal dismissed by Copper v. Denlinger, 363 N.C. 124, 672 S.E.2d 686, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 23 (2009)
Motion granted by Copper v. Denlinger, 675 S.E.2d 35, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 196 (N.C., 2009)
Motion granted by Copper v. Denlinger, 676 S.E.2d 473, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 584 (N.C., 2009)
Motion granted by Copper v. Denlinger, 676 S.E.2d 897, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 553 (N.C., 2009)
Motion to strike denied by, Motion denied by Copper v. Denlinger, 676 S.E.2d 897, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 560 (N.C., 2009)
Reversed by, in part, Review improvidently allowed by, in part Copper v. Denlinger, 363 N.C. 784, 688 S.E.2d 426, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 33 (N.C., Jan. 29, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Durham County. No. 06 CVS 3257.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part; reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Durham County Superior Court (North Carolina) dismissed an action brought by appellants, former and current minority students, under N.C. R. Civ. P. 12(b)(1) and (b)(6) for lack of subject matter jurisdiction and for failure to state a cause of action against appellees, board of education, school officials, and county officials. The suit challenged the school's disciplinary process on due process and equal protection grounds.


OVERVIEW: A smaller group of students appealed, limiting their claims to those under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983(3), N.C. Const. art. I, §§ 1, 15, 19 and art. IX, § 2, and the Fourteenth Amendment against the superintendent and the board. The court reviewed the merits of the claims because the students' failure to timely file the record of appeal was not jurisdictional; however, sanctions were warranted under N.C. R. App. P. 34(b). It was error to dismiss the short-term suspension claims for failure to exhaust administrative remedies; allegations that a board policy barred appeal sufficiently alleged futility. Dismissal of the supervisory liability claim under § 1983 was proper; there was no allegation that the superintendent had knowledge. Dismissal of the deceased student's futility claim on qualified immunity grounds was error. A reasonable superintendent would have known that procedural due process rights were being violated. The equal protection claims, asserting racial profiling and false accusations of gang membership, were inconsistent with claims that students of other races were accused of gang membership. There were sufficient allegations that the gang policy was unconstitutionally vague.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of all claims except the procedural due process claim brought on behalf of a student who was deceased. The court reversed the dismissal as to the constitutional challenge to the board's policy relating to gangs and gang activity. The court remanded the deceased student's claim and the gang policy claim for further proceedings. The court ordered appellants' counsel to pay the printing costs of the appeal.

New Hanover County Water & Sewer Dist. v. Thompson


NO. COA08-258


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


193 N.C. App. 404; 667 S.E.2d 501; 2008 N.C. App. LEXIS 1816


September 10, 2008, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
October 21, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
New Hanover County. No. 06 CVS 2583.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant landowner challenged a final judgment entered in favor of plaintiff county water and sewer district in the New Hanover County Superior Court (North Carolina) in the district's condemnation action. The trial court determined that the landowner failed to plead or appear in the time allowed by N.C. Gen. Stat. § 40A-46 (2007) regarding the justness of the compensation deposited.


OVERVIEW: The district wanted to obtain an easement for a sewer line along the property line of the landowner's vacant lot. It adopted a resolution authorizing condemnation of sewer utility easements on the property and sent the landowner a notice of action. The district filed a complaint against the landowner. The civil summons incorrectly notified the landowner that he had thirty days to respond to the complaint, rather than 120 days allowed by § 40A-46. The landowner filed an answer, denying that $ 12,000.00 was just compensation for the taking. The court of appeals held that the trial court did not err in awarding final judgment for the district. The trial court's finding that the landowner did not file an answer within 120 days of service of the complaint supported its conclusion of law that $ 12,000 was just compensation. The landowner failed to show that the district abused its power by proceeding to final judgment. The district was not on notice that the landowner appeared in the case until after the 120-day time limit had passed. The landowner received ample notice and opportunity to contest the amount of just compensation. The error in the civil summons did not prejudice him.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals affirmed the trial court's judgment.

469 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Narron


NO. COA08-129


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


193 N.C. App. 76; 666 S.E.2d 860; 2008 N.C. App. LEXIS 1758


August 20, 2008, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
October 7, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by State v. Narron, 363 N.C. 135, 674 S.E.2d 140, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 135 (2009)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Narron v. N.C., 130 S. Ct. 71, 175 L. Ed. 2d 26, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 7014 (U.S., Oct. 5, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Pitt County. No. 07 CRS 50348.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following the denial of his motion to dismiss, a jury in the Pitt County Superior Court (North Carolina) found defendant guilty of impaired driving, in violation of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 20-138.1 (2007). Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that N.C. Gen. Stat. § 20-138.1 (2007) was unconstitutional because the provision that states that "[t]he results of a chemical analysis shall be deemed sufficient evidence to prove a person's alcohol concentration, constituted a mandatory presumption violative of defendant's right to due process under the Fifth and Fourteenth Amendments. The appellate court disagreed, concluding that the challenged provision did not create an evidentiary or factual presumption, but simply stated the standard for prima facie evidence of a defendant's alcohol concentration. The appellate court concluded that the meaning of the phrase "shall be deemed sufficient evidence to prove" was that properly admitted results of a chemical analysis must be treated as prima facie evidence of a defendant's alcohol concentration. In other words, the statute simply authorized the jury to find that the report was what it purported to be - the results of a chemical analysis showing defendant's alcohol concentration. The appellate court further concluded that there was no error in the jury instructions, as they adequately informed the jury of the law as applied to the evidence presented at trial.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

 

470 of 825 DOCUMENTS


Pope v. Rollins (In re Estate of Pope)


NO. COA06-1644


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


192 N.C. App. 321; 666 S.E.2d 140; 2008 N.C. App. LEXIS 1626


September 20, 2007, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
September 2, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Pope v. Rollins (In re Estate of Pope), 363 N.C. 126, 673 S.E.2d 129, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 69 (N.C., Feb. 5, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Wake County. No. 04 E 777.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner widow challenged an order of the Wake County Superior Court (North Carolina), which granted respondents, a decedent's siblings, summary judgment and denied the widow's claim for an elective share under the North Carolina Elective Share Act, N.C. Gen. Stat. § 30-3.1 et seq. (2007).


OVERVIEW: The decedent and siblings entered into a family trust agreement, which named them as trustees. After the decedent died, the siblings transferred the principal of his trust to a charity. The widow filed a petition for elective share under § 30-3.1. The court of appeals held that she failed to establish that the clerk of the superior court did not comply with the procedural requirements of § 30-3.4, which did not preclude resolution of the elective share issues on summary judgment. The widow also failed to show that the superior court applied an improper standard of review. The superior court conducted a de novo review and did not err in affirming the clerk's decision based on its construction of the effective date of the Act, rather than on the clerk's statutory analysis. The assets in the decedent's trust were not part of his total net assets. The decedent did not have legal title within the meaning of § 30-3.2(4)(a). Legal title to the trust assets was held collectively by the trustees. The taxable estate was zero because the trust assets were all transferred to the charity. Therefore, no value was left in the decedent's trust for purposes of calculating his total net assets.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals affirmed the judgment.

City of Asheville v. State


NO. COA07-516


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


192 N.C. App. 1; 665 S.E.2d 103; 2008 N.C. App. LEXIS 1556


February 4, 2008, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
August 19, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by, Appeal dismissed by City of Asheville v. State & Buncombe, 363 N.C. 123, 672 S.E.2d 685, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 121 (N.C., Feb. 5, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Wake County. No. 05 CVS 10743



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff city appealed the order of the Wake County Superior Court (North Carolina), which denied its motion for summary judgment, granted the cross-motions filed by defendants, the State of North Carolina and a county, and dismissed the action. At issue was the city's ability to charge different rates to water customers outside its limits considering 1933 N.C. Sess. Laws 376, 2005 N.C. Sess. Laws 246-47, and N.C. Gen. Stat. § 160A-312 (2007).


OVERVIEW: The session laws and statute at issue were referred to as Sullivan I, Sullivan II, and Sullivan III, respectively. The court concluded that the city was precluded under the doctrine of res judicata from challenging the constitutionality of Sullivan I under any provision of the North Carolina Constitution in the present case since prior litigation had determined the same issue. The court further upheld the constitutionality of Sullivan II and Sullivan III, concluding that neither related to health and sanitation. The court upheld the trial court's determination that the Sullivan Acts were constitutional as a valid exercise of legislative authority and that they were not local acts in violation of N.C. Const. art. II, § 24. The court determined that the city had abandoned several arguments on appeal as a result of failing to brief the arguments properly.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court.

Livesay v. Carolina First Bank


NO. COA07-1578


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


192 N.C. App. 234; 665 S.E.2d 158; 2008 N.C. App. LEXIS 1528


May 21, 2008, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
August 19, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at Livesay v. Carolina First Bank, 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 269 (N.C. Ct. App., Mar. 17, 2009)
Review dismissed by, As moot Livesay v. Carolina First Bank, 363 N.C. 655, 686 S.E.2d 517, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 1119 (2009)
Appeal dismissed by, Review denied by Livesay v. Carolina First Bank, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 1443 (N.C., Nov. 5, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Henderson County. No. 05 CVS 2081.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff trustee challenged an order of the Henderson County Superior Court (North Carolina), which granted partial summary judgment in favor of defendants, the administrator of a decedent's estate and creditors, in the trustee's declaratory judgment action seeking a declaration that trust assets were not subject to the debts of the decedent's estate.


OVERVIEW: The trustee and decedent, as trustors, created a trust for their benefit and for the benefit of their children. Upon the death of either trustor, the trust was to inure to the benefit of the surviving trustor and the children. The administrator and creditors filed a motion for partial summary judgment, arguing that pursuant to the Uniform Trust Act, the trust assets were subject to the creditors' claims because the trust was revocable at the time of the decedent's death. The court of appeals held that under the terms of the trust document, there was no genuine issue of material fact with respect to the revocability of the trust. The trustee did not specifically allege that the trust was not revocable. The trial court did not err in finding that the Act, N.C. Gen. Stat. § 36C-5-505(a)(3) (2007), applied. The decedent died with the power to alter, amend, revoke, or terminate the trust. Therefore, the trust would be included in his gross estate for estate tax purposes. Until the moment of his death, the trust was revocable. The beneficiaries of a revocable trust had no vested rights, merely an expectancy. Therefore, they had no constitutionally protected rights to the trust assets.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals affirmed the judgment.

Stone v. State


NO. COA07-718


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


191 N.C. App. 402; 664 S.E.2d 32; 2008 N.C. App. LEXIS 1485


January 15, 2008, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
August 5, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by, Notice of appeal dismissed by Stone v. State, 363 N.C. 381, 680 S.E.2d 712, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 372 (N.C., June 17, 2009)
Review denied by, Notice of appeal dismissed by Stone v. State, 363 N.C. 381, 680 S.E.2d 711, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 369 (N.C., June 17, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Wake County. No. 02 CVS 007931.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff class of state employees appealed a summary judgment in favor of defendants, a state official and pension board on the employees' claim for writ of mandamus to compel compliance with N.C. Gen. Stat. § 135-8(f)(3). Defendants, the State, its governor and state officials, appealed the summary judgment in favor of the class on its claims for a declaratory judgment under N.C. Const. art. V, § 6(2) and under the Contract Clause.


OVERVIEW: The class sued in response to the governor's Executive Order No. 3 requiring state employers to forward their retirement system contributions for certain months to the state treasury due to a fiscal deficit. The court applied the plain meaning of the term divert in art. V, § 6(2) and found that the prohibition against seizure of funds applied to and included funds appropriated and intended for the system, but not yet deposited therein. The Order violated the Contract Clause because a contractual relationship existed between vested class members and the system. Under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 135-6(i), (m)-(o), the class had a contractual right to have the system funded in an actuarially sound manner; therefore, the right to such funding was a term included in the retirement contracts. By diverting the funds in an actuarially unsound manner, defendants impaired the class' contractual right in violation of the Contract Clause. A balanced budget could have been achieved in another way. The pension board did not have to impose N.C. Gen. Stat. § 135-8(f)(3) (2007) penalties because the employers did not default on their obligation to remit contributions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decisions.

477 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Veazey


NO. COA07-1569


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


191 N.C. App. 181; 662 S.E.2d 683; 2008 N.C. App. LEXIS 1228


May 21, 2008, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
July 1, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at State v. Veazey, 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 2234 (N.C. Ct. App., Dec. 8, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Stokes County. No. 06 CRS 050005.



DISPOSITION: Remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant's suppression motion was denied by the Stokes County Superior Court (North Carolina). He pleaded no contest to driving while impaired. preserving his right to appeal the denial of the suppression motion. After he was sentenced, defendant orally entered a notice of appeal. The trial court issued a written opinion, and voided defendant's oral notice of appeal because it was entered before a final written order was entered.


OVERVIEW: Defendant filed a new notice of appeal. The appellate court held that the trooper testified that the primary purpose of the checkpoint was to find motor vehicle law violations, to find all criminal violations, and to check for drivers' license, registration, and insurance violations. The trial court erred in failing to find the actual primary purpose of the checkpoint and whether it was lawful. The trial court's oral and written findings simply recited two of the trooper's stated purposes. The case was remanded for a finding as to the primary purpose. The strength of the State's interest in conducting the checkpoint was not addressed. Without findings as to the second Brown factor, the findings that the checkpoint was reasonable were not supported. The findings on the third Brown prong alone could not support the conclusion that the checkpoint was not an unreasonable detention. The reason for the conclusion the public interest in the checkpoint outweighed the intrusion on defendant's Fourth Amendment interests was not explained. Finally, there was reasonable suspicion to permit the trooper to detain defendant based on the strong odor of alcohol and defendant's red and glassy eyes.


OUTCOME: The case was remanded to the trial court to issue new findings and conclusions regarding the primary programmatic purpose of the checkpoint. If the trial court determined on remand that the State's primary purpose for the checkpoint was lawful, it had to also issue new findings and conclusions regarding the reasonableness of the checkpoint.

Baxter v. Danny Nicholson, Inc.


NO. COA07-865


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


191 N.C. App. 168; 661 S.E.2d 892; 2008 N.C. App. LEXIS 1149


January 16, 2008, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
June 17, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted by, Stay denied by Baxter v. Danny Nicholson, Inc., 363 N.C. 580, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 723 (2009)
Review granted by, Stay granted by, Motion granted by Baxter v. Danny Nicholson, Inc., 682 S.E.2d 213, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 1112 (N.C., 2009)
Motion denied by Baxter v. Nicholson, 684 S.E.2d 441, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 881 (N.C., 2009)
Motion granted by Baxter v. Nicholson, 684 S.E.2d 443, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 890 (N.C., 2009)
Motion denied by Baxter v. Nicholson, 686 S.E.2d 511, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 1049 (N.C., 2009)
Motion granted by Baxter v. Nicholson, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 1168 (N.C., Nov. 30, 2009)
Reversed by, Remanded by Baxter v. Danny Nicholson, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 202 (N.C., Mar. 12, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
North Carolina. Industrial Commission. I.C. No. 687008



DISPOSITION: Vacated and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employer appealed from an opinion and award of the North Carolina Industrial Commission, which found appellee employee totally disabled, reinstated the employee's temporary total disability benefits, and awarded the employee attorney's fees. Additionally, the employer appealed from an order of the Commission, which denied the employer's motion to vacate and reconsider the opinion and award.


OVERVIEW: A panel consisting of three commissioners, reviewed the competent evidence of record, and one commissioner, with another commissioner concurring, signed an opinion and award. A third commissioner dissented. That same day, the North Carolina Office of the Governor issued a letter informing the concurring commissioner that his term as commissioner had expired and his successor had been appointed. The employer contended that, when the opinion and award was filed, the concurring commissioner was not a qualified commissioner; thus, considering the split opinion, the Commission had no majority vote. On appeal, the court found that, when the opinion was filed, the concurring commissioner was not an officer de jure pursuant to N.C. Const. art. VI, § 10 and N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 128-6 and 128-7 (2007) because his term as commissioner had ended and his successor had been appointed. Therefore, because the commissioner's appointment was void before the opinion and award were entered, and because his concurrence in the opinion and award was not the act of a qualified officer - neither de jure nor de facto - the concurrence was a nullity. The panel was thus evenly split and no majority existed.


OUTCOME: The opinion and award were vacated, and the case was remanded to the Commission.

State v. Sapp


NO. COA07-1135


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


190 N.C. App. 698; 661 S.E.2d 304; 2008 N.C. App. LEXIS 1098


March 6, 2008, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
June 3, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by, Appeal dismissed by State v. Sapp, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 969 (N.C., Oct. 8, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Mecklenburg County. No. 02 CRS-238989, 03 CRS-2597-2603, 03 CRS-2605-2609.



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Mecklenburg County Superior Court (North Carolina) entered final judgments of conviction after a jury found defendant guilty of murder, first-degree burglary, three counts of first-degree rape, two counts of first-degree kidnapping, and five counts of second-degree kidnapping. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and an accomplice entered an apartment in search of money and drugs. The apartment belonged to a man's girlfriend, whose mother, brother, and three young children were present. Both defendant and the accomplice forced the girlfriend to engage in sexual intercourse. The mother, brother, and three young children were confined to a bedroom. The man directed defendant and the accomplice to the items. Defendant then shot and killed the man. Defendant was convicted of, inter alia, murder, rape, and kidnapping. The appellate court found that (1) defendant was properly convicted of three rapes, as two occurred when defendant changed positions between them and the third, which the accomplice committed, was chargeable to defendant under an "acting in concert" theory, (2) three kidnapping charges regarding the young children were properly sought pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-39 (2007) and the jury was properly instructed on them, as the children were younger than 16 and had been restrained, and (3) the State proved for sentencing purposes that defendant's prior Virginia conviction was substantially similar to a Class A1 or Class 1 misdemeanor in North Carolina.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

Chapel Hill Title & Abstract Co. v. Town of Chapel Hill


NO. COA07-1292


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


190 N.C. App. 487; 660 S.E.2d 667; 2008 N.C. App. LEXIS 1023


March 19, 2008, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
May 20, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reversed by, Remanded by Chapel Hill Title & Abstract Co. v. Town of Chapel Hill, 362 N.C. 649, 669 S.E.2d 286, 2008 N.C. LEXIS 971 (N.C., Dec. 12, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Orange County. No. 07 CVS 311.
Chapel Hill Title v. Town of Chapel Hill, 178 N.C. App. 561, 631 S.E.2d 893, 2006 N.C. App. LEXIS 1609 (2006)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Orange County Superior Court (North Carolina) entered a judgment that reversed respondent adjustment board's decision denying petitioners, a vacant lot owner and vacant lot buyers, request for a variance and remanded the case to the adjustment board with instructions to issue the requested variance. The adjustment board, town, and respondent neighboring property owners appealed.


OVERVIEW: The vacant lot owner owned a vacant lot in the town. The lot was subject to an ordinance regulating development in a Resource Conservation District (RCD). A portion of the property lay within the RCD. The vacant lot owner applied for a building permit to construct a house on part of the property located outside the RCD. The town granted the building permit. However, when construction began, the neighboring property owners successfully enjoined the construction as being in violation of certain restrictive covenants. The vacant lot buyers purchased the lot subject to the adjustment board granting a variance to build within the RCD. Eventually, the adjustment board denied the variance request; the trial court reversed. The appellate court found that the adjustment board properly found that its inquiry was limited to whether the RCD ordinance left the lot with no reasonable use pursuant to Chapel Hill, N.C., Land Use Management Ordinance § 3.6.3(j)(2)(A)(1). It noted that the town had previously granted a building permit to construct a house on the property outside the RCD and, thus, the restrictive covenants could not be used to override the adjustment board's variance decision.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the trial court's judgment and remanded the case to the trial court for further proceedings.

 

482 of 825 DOCUMENTS


Petroleum Traders Corp. v. State


NO. COA07-1176


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


190 N.C. App. 542; 660 S.E.2d 662; 2008 N.C. App. LEXIS 1003


April 3, 2008, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
May 20, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Wake County. No. 06 CVS 10405.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, the Governor of North Carolina, state agencies, and state officials, challenged an order of the Wake County Superior Court (North Carolina), which denied their motion to dismiss plaintiff corporation's action, seeking a declaratory judgment that an E-Procurement fee was a tax not enacted by the legislature in violation of N.C. Const. art. II, § 23 and seeking a refund of the taxes it paid under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 105-267 (2007).


OVERVIEW: The corporation sold fuel to the State. The State required vendors to submit bids online using the E-Procurement website. The winner was charged a marketing fee of the contract amount, which was authorized by N.C. Gen. Stat. § 66-58.12 (2007). The corporation claimed that the fee was unconstitutional. Defendants moved to dismiss the action, arguing that it was barred by sovereign immunity. The court of appeals held that the corporation's claims were barred by the defense of sovereign immunity. Defendants were state agencies and officials sued in their official capacities, and they did not expressly waive sovereign immunity. Sovereign immunity was not waived by either the Declaratory Judgment Act, N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 1-253 to 1-267 (2007), or N.C. Gen. Stat. § 105-267 (2007). The North Carolina Supreme Court's holding that sovereign immunity could not prevent a plaintiff from asserting a claim alleging a violation of his rights under the Declaration of Rights of the North Carolina Constitution was limited to alleged violations of personal rights and did not extend to the corporation's complaint. N.C. Const. art. II, § 23 did not articulate any rights, only procedures to be followed.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals reversed the trial court's order.

Wake Cares, Inc. v. Wake County Bd. of Educ.


NO. COA07-810


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


190 N.C. App. 1; 660 S.E.2d 217; 2008 N.C. App. LEXIS 872


January 9, 2008, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
May 6, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Wake County. No. 07 CVS 4020.
Wake Cares, Inc. v. Wake County Sch. Bd., 655 S.E.2d 403, 2007 N.C. LEXIS 1149 (N.C., 2007)



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a declaratory judgment suit brought by plaintiffs, parents and a nonprofit association, defendant board of education (BOE) challenged decisions of the Wake County Superior Court (North Carolina) that denied the BOE's N.C. R. Civ. P. 12 motions that alleged a lack of standing and that ruled that the BOE had no statutory authority to convert traditional calendar schools to mandatory year-round schools without the parents' prior consent.


OVERVIEW: The BOE approved a plan to operate some schools on a year-round basis after giving the parents an opportunity to select a traditional or year-round track for their children. The court found that the parents had alleged sufficient standing to allow their claims under the Declaratory Judgment Act, N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1-254 (2007), because they were directly affected by the plan. Because their children could be reassigned to year-round schools in the future, an actual controversy existed, and a declaratory judgment was appropriate to resolve the controversy. The parents did not have to exhaust administrative remedies because their claims did not fall within the scope of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 115C-369 (2007). However, the BOE had authority under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 115C-47(11) to determine the school calendar under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 115C-84.2 (2007), which did not bar year-round schools. N.C. Gen. Stat. § 115C-1 did not provide a right to equal opportunity to attend a school with a traditional calendar. Under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 115C-366(b), the BOE's decision to assign a student to a year-round school was final subject only to a N.C. Gen. Stat. § 115C-369 (2007) application for reassignment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's decision and remanded for entry of judgment in favor of the BOE.

 

484 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Morgan


NO. COA07-745


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


189 N.C. App. 716; 660 S.E.2d 545; 2008 N.C. App. LEXIS 688


February 5, 2008, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
April 15, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Mecklenburg County. No. 05 CRS 225657.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Mecklenburg County Superior Court (North Carolina) entered a judgment that granted the State's motion to appeal, reinstated a DWI charge against defendant, and remanded the case to the district court for trial after the district court had initially dismissed that charge. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested and charged with DWI. The district court then dismissed his case allegedly because the arresting police officer's arrest affidavit, and affidavit and revocation report, were not properly sworn and notarized since the documents did not state the date on which the notary's commission expired. After a hearing on the State's motion to appeal, the trial court found that the documents contained everything necessary, including the expiration date of the notary's commission. Accordingly, the trial court remanded the case to the district court, but also certified the case for appeal. The appellate court found that the Double Jeopardy Clause of the United States Constitution barred the State's appeal. The appellate court concluded that once the district court dismissed the criminal charge against defendant because the State's evidence was insufficient as a matter of law, double jeopardy barred further prosecution even though the missing expiration date finding appeared to be erroneous. The appellate court added that the double jeopardy principle did not operate to correct errors resulting in a preverdict acquittal, even including an error patently wrong in the law.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the trial court's judgment.

Good Hope Health Sys., L.L.C. v. N.C. HHS


NO. COA05-123-2


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


189 N.C. App. 534; 659 S.E.2d 456; 2008 N.C. App. LEXIS 700



April 15, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed by Good Hope Health Sys. v. N.C. HHS, 362 N.C. 504, 666 S.E.2d 749, 2008 N.C. LEXIS 803 (N.C., Oct. 10, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
North Carolina Department of Health and Human Services. No. 03 DHR 1838.
Good Hope Health Sys., L.L.C. v. N.C. HHS, 360 N.C. 635, 637 S.E.2d 517, 2006 N.C. LEXIS 1191 (2006)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant hospital company filed a full acute care application for a certificate of need (CON) with appellee, the North Carolina Department of Health and Human Services, Division of Facility Services, to build a new replacement hospital in appellant town. The administrative agency denied the application. The hospital company and the town appealed. Appellee hospitals intervened in support of the decision to deny the CON application.


OVERVIEW: The hospital company was formed as a successor to a hospital which was previously granted a CON. The company's CON application sought to relocate all hospital departments to a new facility in another town and to abandon the existing hospital campus. Applying N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 131E-176(16) and 131E-181(a) (2003), the agency determined that the CON application could not be treated as an amendment to the prior CON. Moreover, because the CON application proposed the relocation of two operating rooms and added a third, the CON application provided a new institutional health service under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 131E-176(16)(u), which required the company to comply with the North Carolina State Medical Facilities Plan under N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 131E-176(16)(u) and 131E-183(a) (2003). On appeal, the court found that the agency acted within its authority by treating the application as one for a new institutional health service rather than as a modification to the prior CON. The agency also did not err by concluding that the company failed to carry its burden of demonstrating compliance with the relevant statutory review criteria. Accordingly, the agency did not err by denying the CON application.


OUTCOME: The final agency decision was affirmed.

State v. Tyson


NO. COA07-389


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


189 N.C. App. 408; 658 S.E.2d 285; 2008 N.C. App. LEXIS 652


November 1, 2007, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
April 1, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Beaufort County. Nos. 05 CRS 51171, 4678.



DISPOSITION: Remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction by the Superior Court, Beaufort County (North Carolina) for one count of possession of marijuana with intent to sell or deliver marijuana within 300 feet of a playground and one count of having attained the status of habitual felon. Defendant argued, inter alia, that the trial court erred by denying his motion to dismiss the charges in two files because the charges were encompassed in his prior plea arrangement.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested after he was observed selling marijuana. He pled guilty to prior cocaine charges. The plea agreement did not reference a file number associated with the marijuana charges to be dismissed. It was not clear that either the prosecutor or defendant was aware that two separate criminal files had been created. He was indicted for possession with intent to sell or deliver marijuana within 300 feet of a playground. The State claimed that it had only agreed to dismiss the marijuana charges in one file and not the marijuana charge in another file. The appellate court held that it appeared that defendant's guilty plea to the cocaine charges may have been induced by misrepresentations made by the State. Given the similarity of the alleged offenses and their close temporal proximity, defendant was entitled to an explanation that the State was only agreeing to dismiss the marijuana charges in one file. The State presented substantial evidence that the recreational facility in question contained at least three separate apparatuses. The State presented substantial evidence that defendant had attained the status of habitual felon.


OUTCOME: The trial court committed no error with regard to the issues of defendant's conviction for trafficking within 1000 feet of a park and his status as a habitual offender. The matter was remanded for the trial court to make additional findings of fact and conclusions of law with regard to the issue of his guilty plea and the dismissal of certain charges.

Heatherly v. State


NO. COA06-770


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


189 N.C. App. 213; 658 S.E.2d 11; 2008 N.C. App. LEXIS 530


May 22, 2007, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
March 18, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion granted by Heatherly v. State, 662 S.E.2d 668, 2008 N.C. LEXIS 479 (N.C., 2008)
Affirmed by Heatherly v. State, 363 N.C. 115, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 229 (N.C., Mar. 20, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Wake County. No. 05 CVS 17197.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Wake County Superior Court (North Carolina) entered a judgment that denied a preliminary injunction motion to enjoin defendant lottery supporters from proceeding with implementation of the North Carolina State Lottery Act, N.C. Gen. Stat. § 18C-101 et seq. (Lottery Act). It then held that the Lottery Act was not a revenue bill that had to be enacted under mandated constitutional procedural requirements. Plaintiff lottery objectors appealed.


OVERVIEW: The General Assembly passed a law to create a state lottery. The governor signed it the next day. The State provided money for start-up costs and the lottery commission began setting up operations. More than three months after the law was enacted, the lottery objectors claimed in their declaratory judgment action that the law violated N.C. Const. art. II, § 23, requiring that all revenue bills be enacted according to certain constitutional mandates. The trial court found that the law was not a revenue bill and ordered the lottery objectors to pay litigation costs pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1-263 (2005). The appellate court found that the law was not a revenue bill because it was not enacted to (1) raise money on the credit of the State, (2) pledge the faith of the State directly or indirectly for the payment of any debt, or (3) impose any tax upon the people of the State, as required under N.C. Const. art. II, § 23. It also found no error in the awarding of costs under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1-263 (2005), as the lottery objectors delayed initiating litigation for more than three months after the law was enacted and the commission had started setting up lottery operations.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

City of Wilmington v. Hill


NO. COA07-11


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


189 N.C. App. 173; 657 S.E.2d 670; 2008 N.C. App. LEXIS 385


October 9, 2007, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
March 4, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
New Hanover County. No. 06 CVD 001261.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The New Hanover County District Court (North Carolina) entered a judgment that granted defendant owner's motion to dismiss plaintiff city's action against the owner for not meeting an owner-residency requirement related to the owner's garage apartment he had built, declared part of Wilmington, N.C., Land Development Code § 18-285(g) unconstitutional, and declared citations issued against the owner to be void. The owner appealed.


OVERVIEW: After the owner applied for a permit to build a garage apartment on his property, he was notified that his property violated the city's land development code. Wilmington, N.C., Land Development Code § 18-285(g) required the owner of a garage apartment to reside either in the main residence or the garage apartment. He was later cited for violating Wilmington, N.C., Land Development Code § 18-285(g). Eventually, at a trial de novo, the trial court declared the part of the ordinance he was charged with violating, requiring owner occupancy, to be unconstitutional. It also voided his citations. The appellate court found (1) that the owner was not required to exhaust administrative remedies before filing his court challenge to the ordinance, (2) the part of the ordinance requiring owner occupancy was unconstitutional because it impermissibly regulated his lawful ownership of the property rather than use of it, and (3) Wilmington, N.C., Land Development Code § 18-285(g), to the extent of regulating owner occupancy, was beyond the authority of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 160A-383 (2006) to regulate property, as it did not regulate the health, safety, or general welfare of the property.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

State v. Campbell


NO. COA07-903


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


188 N.C. App. 701; 656 S.E.2d 721; 2008 N.C. App. LEXIS 280


December 13, 2007, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
February 19, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal dismissed by State v. Campbell, 664 S.E.2d 311, 2008 N.C. LEXIS 667 (N.C., June 11, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Orange County. No. 06 CRS 54059.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of an Orange County trial court (North Carolina) in which he was convicted upon entry of his pleas of guilty to possession of burglary tools and possession of drug paraphernalia. Specifically, he appealed from an order denying his motion to suppress.


OVERVIEW: After officer one stopped defendant, who was riding a bicycle in the vicinity of a breaking and entering, she placed him in investigative detention. During a frisk, officer one discovered a flashlight and a knife in defendant's pocket. She believed that defendant could have used the knife to pry open a window. After arresting defendant, the officers found jewelry and tools, including a screwdriver, in defendant's backpack. Affirming the denial of the motion to suppress, the court found that the officers had reasonable suspicion to stop defendant based on his proximity to the location of the crime, the time of day, and the absence of other persons in the area. The officers did not unreasonably seize defendant as there had been prior occasions in which defendant had fled. In addition, officer one had probable cause to arrest defendant for possession of burglary tools under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-55 (2005) because her suspicion that defendant possessed implements of housebreaking was supported by (1) defendant's possession of the flashlight; (2) defendant's possession of the backpack containing unknown items; and (3) the factors supporting the finding of reasonable suspicion.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

Rowlette v. State


NO. COA06-1036


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


188 N.C. App. 712; 656 S.E.2d 619; 2008 N.C. App. LEXIS 284


March 15, 2007, Heard in the Court of Appeals 
February 19, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by, Appeal dismissed by Rowlette v. State, 362 N.C. 474, 666 S.E.2d 487, 2008 N.C. LEXIS 710 (N.C., Aug. 26, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Wake County. No. 05 CVS 2677.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owners sought review of an order entered by the Wake County Superior Court (North Carolina), which dismissed the owners' complaint alleging that defendants, the State and its treasurer, improperly retained interest or income on property of the owners that defendants had in their custody under the North Carolina Unclaimed Property Act, N.C. Gen. Stat. § 116B-51 et seq. (2003).


OVERVIEW: The owners claimed that the retention of the interest or income on their property by defendants pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 116B-64 (2003) constituted a taking without just compensation in violation of N.C. Const. art. I, § 19 and the Fifth and Fourteenth Amendments of the United States Constitution. The trial court dismissed the claims pursuant to N.C. R. Civ. P. 12(b)(1) and (6). On appeal, the court affirmed. Although no United States or North Carolina Supreme Court case had addressed the exact issue, the court noted that cases cited by the owners dealt with property that unquestionably belonged to identified owners. However, the court found that case law from other states interpreting their unclaimed property acts had held that because the owners' action in failing to make any use of the property at issue, rather than the action of the state, caused the lapse of the property right, no taking occurred that required compensation. Pursuant to the Act, the State did not undertake any overt action to take private property from the owners.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order.
